CREDE ANMD PROJEc SUMMARY
Borrower:   People's Republic of China
Beneficiay: Ministry of Water Resources, the provinces of Gansu, Shaanxi, and Shanxi
and the autonomous region of Inner Mongolia
Amount:    SDR 106.3 million ($150 million equivalent)
Terms:      Standard, with 35 years' maturity
Objectives and
Description: The primary objective of the project is to increase agricultural production
and incomes on 15,600 km2 of land in the Loess Plateau in nine tributary
watersheds of the Yellow River. A secondary objective-to reduce sediment
inflows to the Yellow River-is achieved by locating the project areas in the
parts of the Basin with severe soil erosion. Factors such as poverty level,
strong leadership and commitment at the local government level, and
development potential were also considered in the selection of project areas.
The project will (a) create sustainable crop production on high-yielding
level farmland and thereby replace the areas devoted to crops on erodible
slope lands, (b) plant the slope lands to a range of trees, shrubs and grasses
for land stabilization and the production of much-needed fuel, timber and
fodder, and (c) substantally reduce sediment runoff from slope lands and
gullies in the nine watersheds. The project will include 83,200 ha of
terraces; several thousand sediment control dams; 5,500 ha of small-scale
irrigation; 270,000 ha of commercial and land stabilizing trees and shrubs;
155,000 ha of pasture; 27,000 ha of fruit and nut orchards; and an
institutional development component including training, technology transfer,
monitonng and evaluation, and a research program to tackle soil and water
conservation and production issues specific to the Loess Plateau.
Benefits:   (a) Poverty Alleviation. Per capita incomes in the project area are mostly
below the poverty line (about $35 per year) and nearly 30 percent of the
population is iliterate or semi-literate. The project attacks poverty at its
core, as virtually no sources of income other than from agriculture are
within reach for the 1.2 million people in the project area. Incomes will be
This document bas a resricted distribution and may be used by recipients only in the pefbnan of ther
official duties. Its contents may not othivise be disclosed wnthout World Bak authrzaton.



-ii-
more than doubled as has been demonstated in many villages where
comprehensive control measures were implemented.
(b) Environment. Large areas in the project area are severely degraded
and current agricultural practices are unsustainable. More than 100,000 ha
of presently cultivated land will be taken out of production and, together
with an additional 270,000 ha of mainly wasteland, planted with a mix of
local tree, shrub and grass species. This will not only significantly reduce
erosion, but also improve the microclimate in the now often desert-like
gullies. The land use pattern witl significantly change under the project.
The annual crop area will drop from 28 percent to 21 percent of the total
area, while perennials will increase from 37 percent to 54 percent. The
proportion of wasteland will be reduced from 28 percent to 17 percent.
Improved livestock management will help to reduce the presswe on the
existing grasnd areas. After comprehensive watershed treatment the
annual sediment discharge from the project area will be reduced by half,
contnbuting substantially to an improved sediment regime of the Yellow
River.
(c) nt_al Produ:tion. The project will raise grain output from
427,000 tons to 660,000 tons, and fruit production will increase from
80,000 tons to 320,000 tons. Per capita grain output will increase from
357 kg to 507 kg and per capita incomes will increase from Y 360 to
Y 900. This will be achieved despite the significant reduction in the
planted area as a result of improved water retention and improved cropping
pa.ers.
The project has an overall economic rate of return of 19 percent and will
benefit about 280,000 households directly. The provinces of Henan and
Shandong will benefit from sediment reduction.
Risks:      The project is based on years of Chinese experience in soil conservation and
agriculture on the Loess Plateau. It is also based on the careful analysis of
the World Food Program Mlzhi Project in Shaanxi and the Bank-financed
component in Dingxi county, Gansu, which from all accounts have been
su ful. All components have been exhaustively tested, thus in its
technical aspects the project has few serious risks. On-farm production and
the establishment of trees, shrubs and pasture are, however, susceptible to
drought.  Potential project management risks will be addrssed by
strehening project management under the project.

1. BACKGROUND
A. INTRODUCrON
1.1        The Government of China has requested the International Development
Association (IDA) to assist in financing a watershed rehabilitation project in the
autonomous region of Inner Mongolia and the three provinces of Shanxi, Shaamd and
Gansu. The primary objecdve of the project is to increase agrdultural production and
incomes on 15,600 kin2 of land in the Loess Plateau in tibutar watersheds of the Yellow
River. A secondary objective, which is to reduce sediment inflows to the Yellow River,
will be achieved by locating the project areas in the parts of the Basin with severe soil
erosion. The project will (a) create sustainable crop production on high-yielding level
frmland and thereby replace the areas devoted to crops on erodible slope lands, (b) plant
the slope lands to a range of trees, shrubs and grasses for the land stabilizaion and
production of much-needed fuel, timber and fodder, and (c) subst ntally reduce sedment
runoff from slope lands and gullies in the nine waersheds through the constucton of
sediment retention dams.
1.2        The project was prepared by the provincial water conservancy bureaus,
under the guidance of the Ministry of Water Resources (MWR), and the proposed project
components have been successfully tested and implemented on a large scale in similar areas
of the plateau. The new element which has been introduced in recent years is the
comprehensive and intgrted planning of individual watersheds in close consultation with
the beneficiaries in the villages. An IDA preappraisal mission visited the project areas in
August 1993 and appraisal took place in October/November 1993.
B. Tim AGRICULTu   SEMcro
1.3        Agricuture in China-including crops, livestock, forestry and fisheies-
provides sustenance for about 1.2 bilion people; it is an important source of income for
some 190 million farm families and accounts for about 30 percent of the country's gro
dom'nstic product (GDP). Only about 137 million ha of China's land area of 960 million
ha are arable. Food grains oocupy about 70 percent of total cropland and accout for
about one third of the value of agicultural output. Intensive farming bas allowed China
to meet the basic food requirements of its population, about 22 percent of the world's total,
and to produce a wide variety of cash and commercial crops, from less than 11 percent of
the world's arable land.
1.4        China's rural reform program, initiated in 1978, has strongly stimuated the
agdcuttural sector. This program produced impressive growth and structual change by
introducing the production responsibility system, increasing producer prices, and  anding



-2 -
the role of the free market. State procumiment prices for grain almost doubled from 1938
to 1993. China is moving towards the full liberalization of grain markets. Sales prices
are now set near free market prices and all dirc';t government subsidies to producers have
been eliminated. State procurement prices consist of a protection price, which is defined
as the prce necessary to operate state grain procurement without subsidies, plus a subsidy
to replace the previous provision of subsidized ferlr and diesel oil in return for gain
cozVract fulfillment; a system which was eliminated nationally in 1993. However, state
procurement quotas have not been fuily eliminated, even though payments are now in
principle at the market price. Some procurement obligations have been maintained in order
to ensure that state grain supplies are adequate to meet sales obligations and to provide
farmers with a secure marketing outlet while markets develop. Grain officials stil feel that
without state procurement, farmers accustomed to store a}l surplus production would be
reluctant to sell grain, while farmers who frequently market grain will fear market
instability. Thus, the procurement quota is seen both as a guarantee and an obligation.
This remaining vestige of the planning system reflects a continued fe3r of market failure,
especially in remote regions. As farmers become accustomed to markets, and planners
gain confidence in the overall balance of grain supply and demand, phasing out of this
system is the next logical step, as has been demonstrated in some large provinces (Sichuan,
Guangdong). A cntical next step for the central government will be to allow regions and
even whole provinces to reduce grain production and rely on grain inflows ard to allow
poorer areas to move from self-sufficiency in grain and instead diversify and specializ in
cash crop:. The Government is now considering a floor price for grain to maintain
incentives for grain production.
1.5        Input pnce increases have outpaced those of output prices, and per ha
production cost of grains increased at 9 percent annually from 1982 to 1991, while net
etuS increased only 4.4 percent. This combined with the fact that industral growth has
outaced agricultural growth by a factr of three to five in recent years has led to a
substantally lower growth of incomes of the rura population. The ratio of per capita
income between urban and rural populations increased by over 15 percent between 1988
and 1990 from 2.4:1 to 2.8:1, and this trend is accelerating.
1.6         China's gross value of agricultural output (GVAO) increased by 83 percent
in real terms during the 1978-90 period (6 percent per annum), substantially faster than
dunng the previous 25-year perod (3 percent per annum). While significant growth in
grain production was achieved in the early part of the period (peaking at 407 million tons
in 1984), stagnating grain production has been the Govemment's central concern in later
years with production rangig between 379 and 402 million tons during the 1985-88 period
as farmers substituted other crops for grain. Recently, however, grain production appears
to have risen to a new plateau, with 446 million tons in 1990 (a year of excelent weather),
435 million tons in 1991, and 442 million tons in 1992, despite severe floods in the
Yangtze River Basin and in Northeast China.
1.7        A maor factor in this success has been China's achievements in plant
breeding-China was the first country to develop hybrid rice-backed up by the vast
network of local seed production, extension, and distribution which put new seeds and



-3 -
technology into the hands of the masses of farmers. Nearly 30 percent of the rice now
planted in China is hybrid, as well as virualy all of the corn, while most of the wheats
have high genetic performance potential for the varous agrocimatic regions in which they
are grown. The Government's strategy to increase grain production aims to expand the
supply of chemical fertilizers and other inputs, increase state investment in agriculture,
mainly in irrigation rehabiitation and expansion, and introduce further reforms in grain
procurement, distribution, prices and subsidies. Prices paid by urban residents for
government supplied flour and oils, unchanged for 30 years, were raised sharply in 1991
and 1992. Grain commodity exchanges for al major grains have been established in a
number of Chinese cities. Continuing reform aims to further liberalize grain production,
open the intemra grain trade to al interested units and individuals, commercialize grain
distribution, and establish a futures market in grain.
1.8        An important objective in China's agricultural strategy is to improve
productivity in rainfed areas, particularly in the arid and semi-arid zones accounting for
30 percent of the total amble land. While productivity of China's irrigated areas is uow
mong the highest in the world, progress in dryland agriculture has lagged behind. In fact,
in several predominantly rainfed regions in northwest and southwest China, poor resource
endowments and high population pressure combined with unsustainable agricultural
practices have resulted in widespread poverty that has remained difficult to deal with over
the past decades. The central and provincial governments are currently preparing
investment programs for Bank financing in both Regions, based on successful experience
of earlier and ongoing projects, including those supported by the Bank and other extna
support agencies such as WFP and FAO.
C. SoIL EROsioN iN CHNA
1.9        The total land area in China subject to erosion is estimated to be 147 million
hectares (16 percent of the total land area); an estimated 5 billion tons of topsoil are eroded
annually. The Chinese estimate that the losses of key nutrints from this process are
nearly equivalent to the nation's total annual ferlizer production. Historically, soil
erosion problems have been most severe along the upper-middle Yellow River, wifth
roughly one third of the nation's total erosion taking place along the Yellow River alone.
Despite efforts to counteract soil erosion since the 1950s, erosion problems have
intensified, with an estimated 30 percent more land affected by erosion today than in 1949.
Other areas of particular concern are (a) along the Yangtze River, in which erosion is
estimated to have doubled since the 1950s, and (b) in Sichuan Province, in which erosion
is estimated to have more than quadrupled since the 1950s. In south China, where soils
are generally thinner, susce'tibility to erosion-caused productivity losses is much higher.

D. RUAL POVERTY
1.10       While absolute paverty in China is low among the urban population, it is
restricted almost entdrely to resource constained remote upland areas.-I The incidence
of povety in China fell from roughly a tid of the population in 1978 to less than one
tenth in 1985 and since has remained roughly constant at around 9 percent. Absolute
poverty in urban areas is on the order of 0.4 percent of the registerd population while in
the rural areas, it is betwenn 11 and 12 percent. The decline from 1978-85 coincided with
the implementation of the producuon reWonsibility system and the increased application
of improved agricultuWal techniques which resulted in rapid productivity gains. The
majority of the decline so induced was among the rural poor in less remote and less hilly
areas. The rapid reductions through agricultural growth mostly finished by the end of
1984. The residual poor have remained trapped in more remote upland areas where
making such gains is much more difficult.
1.11       Chinese authorities see a special challenge in developing the remote,
resoure-deicient areas in northern, northwesten and southwestern China including the
provinces of the Loess Plateau region. The Loess Plateau agricltural environment has
limited production potential due to dlimatic constraints and extensive environmental
degadation. Agricul  product   and incomes were further diminished in the 1960s
and 1970s when Loess Plateau farmers were encouraged to switch from semi-palism
to grain cultivation on marginal land as part of a national policy to achieve local self-
sufficiency in grain. Furherm    the population growth and consequent reduction in per
capita area of amble land reduces the likelihood of the resource base meeting minimum
rquimets for farm outputs and income. Relef finds and grain sold at subsidized prices
ar provided to the poor to hp term meet minimum food requirements. Social services
to the rual poor receive Little outside help. Village schools will largely depend on their
own limited resources.
1.12       Based on 1992 statistics, (see Annex 1, Table 7), the rural per capita income
in the project areas was only Y 305 which is about 52 percent of the project provincial
average of Y 587 and less than 39 percent of the national rural average of Y 784. More
than half of the rural population lived below the poverty line of Y 300 per carta per
annum. Birth rates, population growth rates and illitacy rates are all substantally higher
while the population of school-age children enrolled was lower than the national and
provincial averages. Unfivorable natural conditions (insufficient water supply, and
ersion), frequnt namal disasters (drought, wind storm, and sand storms), inconvenient
transrtation facilities, poor education and public health facilities, infectious and endemic
diseases (fluoride toxicosis, hyperthyroidsm) are among the main causes of the severe
poverty in the area.
1.13       In the long run, sabizing the envment and creafing an ecologically
sustainable agriultual system-at as high a lvel of production as the soils and climate

1.14       IlDA support for the project will fiurher the key elements of the

F. BAK GROUP LEING FOR WATS     RA AON
1.15       IDA bas financed two projects in China with distinctive watershed
rbilitation components. Inestig lessons bave been learned from the soil and water
conseraon component of the Gansu Provincial Development Project which focused an
the development of poor areas in Dingxi County (the Guanxuan River Basin) on the LIess
Plateau. The implementation record of this component has been excellent and bas imvolved
local governnent agencies at all levels-province, prefecture, county, townsi and village.
Close consutation with the benefiiaries was a key to success, whfle the strong focus on
high technical standards and improved technology has led to physical implemention
standards above those estimated at apprisd. The Red Soils Project in Southern China also
was successfully based on watshed development activity, although constaints and
solutions differ from those on the Loess Plateau. Farmers in the red soils area have to
cope with low soil fertility, but have sufficient water resources availble, as opposed to the
uaton on the Loess Plateau where the production potental of the basically fertile soils
is underutilized as a result of limited soil moisture.

2. THE PROJECT AREAS
A. THE LOESS PLATEAU
2.1        The Loess Plateau covers an area of some 640,000 km2 in the upper and
middle sections of the drainage basin of the Yellow River. It takes its name from the
German word loess which is used to describe soils deposited by wind action. The Chinese
term for loess is huang tu, or yellow soil. Geologically the Loess Plateau is recent, with
the older deposits dating back to the Pleistocene Epoch, about 2.4 mlldion years ago, and
the newer deposits having been laid down between 100,000 to 10,000 years ago. Deposits
range in depth from 100 to 300 m. Erosion in the form of deep gulies is a prominent
charactristic of the Loess Plateau, and it is this erosion which produces the vast quantity
of sediment caried by the Yellow River. Over the centunes there have been peaks and
troughs in human activity and these have had some effect on rates of deforestaion and soil
erosion.
2.2         One current view is that poverty is both cause and result of the ecological
deterioration on the plateau; a direct consequence of wilfil deforestation and indiscrimina
land reclamation and cultivation. Yet, erosion had already occurred several hundred
thousand years ago on the plateau. It is estimated that the annual sediment load in the
Yellow River 5,000 years ago was already in the range of 1 billion tons as compared to
1.5 billion tons annual average during this centry. This would indicate that the plateau
basically had developed its current shape of steep gullies and deep dissection before
significant number of humans entered the scene. Human damage to the vegetation cover
began about 4,000 years ago, when the plateau was still covered with grass, shrubs or trees
depending on the rainfall gradient. During the Tang dynasty, about 1,200 years ago, Xi'an
was a political and cltural center and an estimated 2 million people lived in the area.
Since then, popuation pressre has drastically increased (nearly 70 million today) and
already before the tum of the century the nal vegetation cover had decreased to about
10 percent. Thus, it would appear that the basic landform is of geologic origin, but the
process of erosion has been accelatd by human intervention. Some Chinese experts see
the erosion of the plateau primarly as a geologic process that has been going on long
before human settlement, and restoring the vegetation alone would not be sufficient to
significantly reduce the sediment flow into the Yellow River. Assuming 50-70 percent
effectiveness of a well established vegetation cover, revegetatton would result in about
20 percent erosion control in a typical watershed. Terraces directly reduce surface erosion
by 95 percent. But they also retain 90 percent of the runoff water, thus, significandy
reducing wall erosion in the gullies and total sediment runoff. Most remaning sediment
can be trapped in gullies by a series of silt retention dams.



- 7-
2.3         The regon is divided into three geomorphological zones called the Shaui
platform, Erdos platform and Longxi basin. The natural vegetaton covers four belts
including deciduous broadleaf forest, forest praire, desert prarie and desert srppe from
southeast to northwest. The area hosts more tban 860 plant species, including about 300
woody plants. Annual precipitation, mostly in the form of rain, ranges from 200 mm in
the north to 550 mm in the south. Over 60 percent falls in the months of July, August and
September often in the form of short intense storms (Ann  - 1 Table 2). Evaporation
ranges from 1,500 to 2,000 mm. Water resources are not only deficient in this area but
also unevenly distributed between regions, years and withn a year. Runoff cha i
in the project areas are summarized as follows: uneven regional distribution ranging from
6,100 n3 in Hequ county to 55,000 m'ekm2 in the Weifen watershed. The diference
between the maximum and the minimum annual runoff can be more than 10 tmes in the
same watershed. Runoff during the flood season usually takes up more than 80 percent
of the total annual runoff. Runoff in the flood season has a sigificantly higher sediment
concentration, of up to 600 kg per m3 of water as compared to the average of 50 kg/n.
2.4         The region receives an average annual sunlight period of 2,000 to 3,000
hours in most areas (Anx 1, Table 1). The effective accumulated temperature hih  or
equal to 100 cengrade is 2,500 to 4,5000, thus meeting the needs of agricuual
production. The average annual tempeture is below 10° centigrade. In summer the
temperae can reach 35C, and in winter it often falls below -1OC.1/ The harsh
climate, low rainfall, and difficult terain present problenms for the development of
sustainable agriculture. However, in recent years, China has made considle progess
in evolving, and testing on a large scale, a range of technical and social solutions to the
development of the Loess Plateau that are economically sound and envrnmentaly
sustnable.
2.5         The Loess Plateau shows a variety of landforms. The commonest landforms
and the most typical of the plateau are the 'gullied hill" and the "gullied plteau.a Wind-
blown sands and arid grasslands are other landforms found in the low rainfall areas of the
northern part of the plateau. Gullied hilly loess consists of rounded hills of coademble
height with a high density of steep-sided gulies showing evidence of active erosion. The
gullied plateau landform is found where the loess was laid down in the form of flat terain
that has been dissected by gllies. The arid gasslands also show relief in the form of
rounded hills but there is less erosion, due to the lack of rainfll. Wind-blown sands have
the general appearance of desert terrain, and until control measures were introduced in
recent years the desert area was expanding.
2.6         Loess soil has the appearance of soft rock. It can be easily excavated by
hand and caves cut in the loess house many of the inhabitants of the plateau. Loess can
be cut to steep slopes that are almost vertical and remain stable for many years if they are
not exposed to noning water. This ch istic can be seen in road cuts and in the
1/    The number of frt-fiee days nmges f&m 130 in Shanxi to 200 days in Shexi.



- 8 -
terraces built to combat soil erosion. It is the susceptibility of the loess to water erosion
that explains the distinctive gullies of the plateau. Gullies tend to erode into steep unstable
slopes and, during floods, the sides of the gullies are undercut and landslides are one of
the main mechanisms of soil erosion. The main component of loess soils (maldng up 50-
60 percent) is silty loam with loose structure and highly soluble calcium carbonate content.
2.7         Short intense storms produce most of the soil erosion. There we also
significant areal variations in the rates of erosion from the Loess Plateau (Annex1,
Table 3). The low rainfibl areas are less productive of sediment because of the low
frequency of storms. The higher rainfall areas are also relatively less prone to erosion
because there is sufficient rain to promote a protective vegetative cover. The highest rates
of erosion are found in areas where the rainfall is on the order of 400 nun; these areas also
have the highest proportion of the coarse sediment that creates problems in the Yellow
River and its tributaries. Sediment runoff from an area is expressed as tons/km2/year (the
sediment modulus) and is averaged over a number of years because there is a wide
variation in soil erosion from year to year. The parts of the plateau with the highest
sediment runoff have a modulus of about 20,000. This is, however, an average of runoff
from a wide variety of terrain and land use. Researchers have found that in the gullied
hilly loess, about 70 percent of the soil loss is from the gullies and about 30 percent from
the surrounding land. An indication of the complexity of the topography is given by data
from the Malian project area in Gansu: the density of gullies is 2 km/kni2, with 1,900
gullies of more than 1 km in length, and 42 percent of the land has a slope of over 25.
2.8         Since the 1950s soil and water consevation on the Loess Plateau has made
significant progress and large areas have been controlled. Untl 1989, 163,000 km2 have
been primarily controlled in which over 3 million ha were terTaced, 870,000 ha of damland
and floodplains established, 7.5 million ha of afforestation completed, 2 million ha of
grassland sown and more than 3 million small-scale soil and water conservation works such
as water-holding cellars, ponds, check dams and gully head protection works constcted.
More than 2,000 small watersheds have been treated. These meaes have made a
significant impact in reducing sediment flow into the Yellow River. Based on observations
at Xanxian Hydrographical station, the annual average sediment tanport during the last
20 years has been about 500 million tons less than during the 20 years before. Although
this was partly due to lower rainfall levels, the annual sediment yield was reduced by 250
million tons at the upper Sanmenxia station. Big tributaries such as the Wuding river, the
Fen nver, the Xingxui nver, the Dahe river and the Sanchuang river after comprehensive
treatment have shown 50 percent sediment reduction.
2.9         With the long cold winter, crops can be grown only between April and
October. Much of the cropland is devoted to cereals such as millet and corn, and oil crops
such as sunflower, rape seed and castor. Vegetables, melons and tobacco are widely
grown as a cash crop. High-quality fruit can be produced in the area and apple and pear
orchards have been established at a rapid pace in recent years. With the establishment of
free markets throughout the area, vegetables are grown where water is available.
livestock raising is an important part of the farming system, especially in Inner Mongolia
where sheep raising is a specialty. Except for the wind-blown sands of Inner Mongolia,



- 9 -
the soils of the Loess Plateau are suitable for a wide range of crops. The alluvial plains
of the larger rivers of the Loess Plateau support surface and groundwater irrigation.
Elsewhere opportunities for irrigation are limited, and generaly confined to areas that can
be supplied from reservoirs formed by dams built in the gullies.
B. DEVEOPMENT STRATEGY
2.10        Since the creation of the PRC, there have been several campaigns, especially
in the 1970s, to terrace the slope lands and to increase the vegetation cover on the plateau.
At the same time thousands of dams have been built to intercept sediment runoff in the
gullies. These measures were aimed pnmarily at erosion control and little attention was
given by planners or farmers to optimizing agncultural production and mamizing farm
incomes.
2.11        The Government's development strategy for the Loess Plateau has been
modified in recent years. It now recognizes that soil conservation is compatible with
sustainable and productive agriculture and that they are mutually reinforcng. Thus, a key
element in this development statey  is the comprehensive and integrated planning of
individual watersheds. The objective is to (a) create sustinable production of field crops
and orchards on high-yielding level farmland and thereby replace the areas devoted to
crops on erodible slope lands, and (b) plant the slope lands to a range of trees, shrubs and
grasses for the production of fuel, timber and fodder. The area devoted to crops is
reduced but the per ha productivity is greatly increased by proven techniques of soil and
water conservation. While the watershed is the basic physical unit (often divided into
subwatersheds) the village is the basic social unit, and the success of the integrated
approach depends on close consultation with village leaders and residents. This integrated
and comprehensive aproach has been successfillly tested and implemented on a fairly large
scale in a number of watersheds in the plateau and has proved to be sustainable.
2.12        While this form of land development helps to arrest soil erosion, the large
volume of gully erosion can only be reduced by directly interepting the sediment by check
dams. The vast quantity of sediment canried by the Yellow River creates major problems
in the managementof the river in its lower reaches. The river bed is rising at a rate of
about one meter every ten years and, therefore the flood protection dikes have to be
frequently raised and strengthened at great cost and increasing hazard. Also, the high
sediment content of the rver in the monsoon season causes silting up of imgation canals
which often have to be closed during periods of peak sediment flow. Of the approimaty
640,00()0 km2 of the entire Loess Plateau, 80,000 km2 produce 60 percent of the annual 600
million tons of coarse sediment carried by the river (it is the coarse sediment which causes
the problem in the lower reaches). Given the urgency of the sediment problem in the
lower reach, the Govement has adopted an approach that can be implemented at a fast
pace and at lower cost per ha than comprehensive watershed development. Such an
approach involves the constuction of silt retention dams on the primary and secondary
gullies draining the Loess Plateau in the areas of high sediment runoff. The reservoir
areas of the dams are uninhabited since the land consists of eroded gullies. The dams
would fill with sediment in five to ten years in most locations, but their height can be
easily raised to increase storage at a ftaction of their orginal cost.



- 10-

populion density is 80 persons/kn2, per capita land and cultivated land is 1.3 ha and

unit output is 1.2 tons per ha. Per capita grain output and net income are 360 kg and

Shanxi
2.15        The ftree project areas in Shanxi Province, Hequ-Baode-Pinguan (HBP),
Weifen and Xinshui, are in the poorest and most heavily eroded parts of the province.
HBP and Weifen have the gullied hill land form, and Xinshui has mostly the guilied
phteau land fonn. Only 30 percent of the land is cultivated. Rainfall averages 410 mm
in HBP, 500 mm in Weifen and 590 mm in Xinshui. There is no natural forest in the
project areas. Afforestation carried out since 1950 has increased the vegetative cover from
less than 3 percent to 18 percent, but much of this area is of poor quality trees and shrubs.
Annual crops take up 29 percent, but some of this land is steep and prone to erosion.
Unused land covers 45 percent of the areas, mostly gully sides, and steep uncultivated
slopes. About 50 percent of this land is suitable for afforestation using techniques
proposed for the project.  The project areas have good potential for substantial
improvement in crop yields through terracing of slope land, and for orchards and more
productive tree and shrub plantaions. The prefecture capitals and county towns in the
project area are all served by highways, and most of the villages are reachable by motor
vehicles. A new raiiway close to completion will connect the HBP area to the provincial
capital of Taiyuan.
Inner Mongolia
2.16        The three wateds in Inner Mongolia, those of the Hantai, Hashila and
Husitai Rivers, are all right bank tributaries of the Yellow River. They have more varied
land forms than the areas in the other  reeprovinces. Each of the rivers rises in an area
of gullied hilly plateau, traverses tracts of wind-blown sand and then flows across an
alluvial fan before entering the Yellow River. The alluvial fans are higher than the
surrounding land, and dikes have been built to contain the rivers dunng floods. These
dikes have served their purpose, but in doing so they have caused the rivers to build up
their beds between the dikes. Thus, the dikes have to be raised periodically at considerable
cost and they are now reaching a height where they are vulnerable to breaches in a high
flood. The only lasting solution to this problem is to reduce floods and sediment runoff
through integrated development of the catchment areas. The check dams built as a part
of this development will also reduce the magnitude and frequency of floods. Where the
nvers cross the sandy areas, warping will be used to create land (para. 3.14). The
population of the Inner Mongolia watrsheds shown in Table 2.1 is for the catchment areas
of the rivers, but a much larger populaton will benefit from the reduction in flood rik
Rainfall in these areas ranges from 300 to 400 -m.

is typical of the gullied hill land form. The project areas are close to several examples of

3. THE PROJECT
A. PROJEcr OBIECnvEs AN DESIGN
3.1         The primary objective of the project is to increase agricultural production
and incomes on 15,600 kmn2 of land in the Loess Plateau in nine tributary watersheds of
the Yellow River. A secondary objective-to reduce sediment inflows to the Yellow River
-is achieved by locating the project areas in the parts of the Basin with severe soil
erosion. Factors such as poverty level, strong leadership and commitment at the local
government level and development potential were also considered in the selection of project
areas. The project will (a) create sustainable crop production on high-yielding level
farmland and thereby replace the areas devoted to crps on erodible slope lands, (b) plant
the slope lands to a range of trees, shrubs and grasses for land stabilization and the
production of fuel, timber and fodder, and (c) substantally reduce sediment runoff from
slope lands and gullies.
3.2         The project has been prepared in considerable detail and examples of the
proposed project components have been successfully tested and implemented on a large
scale in similar areas of the plateau. The new element which has been introduced in recent
years is the comprehensive and integrated planning of individual watersheds in close
consultation with the beneficiaries in the villages. Many of the existing terraces, built in
the 1970s under large-scale campaigns, have been abandoned and left unused because they
were not parts of integmted, village-based production systems. For the same reason other
measures such as the planting of trees, shrubs, and grassland establishment have been only
partialy successful.
B. PRojEcr DESCRIPTION
3.3         The project has two main components:
(a) the construction of land development and erosion control works on
15,600 km2 in Inner Mongolia, Shanxi, Shaanxi, and Gansu; and
(b) institutional development programs to suppor training, technology transfer
and research.
Land Development and Erosion Control Works
3.4         Terrang is planned for 83,200 ha and will be anied out on slope lands
with slopes between 5° and 25. The conversion of slope lands to terraces serves a
number of purposes. First, it creates a better environment for crop producdon through



- 14 -
moisture conservation; in a year of average rainfall, grain yields on terraces are more than
three times higher than on slope lands. Second, teraced land is more resistant to
damaging soil erosion during intense storms than slope land. And third, sediment runoff
is much lower from terraces than from sloped land. Generally, teraces are confined to
lands where slopes are less than 20°, but where there is a shortage of crop land, terraces
are built on slopes up to 250. Over most of the Loess Plateau the soil is deep and is
therefore well suited to terracing, but the topsoil is often set aside and replaced upon
completion of the terrace. For a typical terrace on a ISO slope, the terrace would be about
8 m wide, the riser would be 2 m high, and the earthwork quantity per hectare would be
around 3,000 cubic meters. The construction cost ranges from Y 2,000/ha to Y 5,000/ha
depending on the slope. Uniform standards for planning, design and construction have
been developed in recent years and will be adopted throughout the project. The labor
required for the construction of terraces is gene-ally available in the project areas. But,
when labor is in short supply, such as in the peak harvest season, construction using
bulldozers is competitive in terms of costs.
3.5         Afforestation is planned for 270,000 ha. This will consist of shrubs and
trees that have been found suitable for the various soils and topography found in the project
areas. Afforestation will be contracted out to farmers for tending and for controlled
grzing. This component will increase the forest cover in the project areas from
14 percent to 38 percent and will significantly reduce soil erosion. It will help to relieve
a shortage of timber, forage, fodder and fuel wood in the project areas. Participating
vllages will develop an area management plan and assign individuals to control livestock
grzing, particularly during the first few years after planting; this system has worked well
in recent years. Some of the past programs of afforestation have produced mixed results;
there have been some notable successes, but in some areas survival rates and growth rates
have been low. Current practice is more scientific and solidly based on field trials. More
attention is now paid to suiting plantings to location, soils, slope, and exposure. Seedling
quality, tending and protection has been improved by the production responsibility system
which gives individual farmers a financial stake in the survival of plantngs.
3.6         Afforestation is based on detailed plans involving a classification system
taking into account all factors relevant to species selection. Trees will be planted on about
100,000 ha. Tree species commonly used include native drought-resistant species such as
Chinese pine, poplar, black locust, willow, larch and arborvitae. Commercial trees include
apricot, walnut, and mulberry. Poplar and willow are planted on the top and edges of
plateaus, Chinese pine or north-facing slopes, black locust and arborvitae on the middle
and lower part of south-facing slopes. Larch is planted in mountainous areas with altitudes
over 1,500 meters. The success of afforestation depends on good quality seedlings; these
will be provided by nurseries set up in each county with about 1 ha of nursery for each
1,000 ha of afforestation.
3.7         Shrubs will be planted on about 163,400 ha, including 74,200 ha of caragana
(Caragaa korshshnii), 56,100 ha of seabuckthorn (fippophae rhamnoides), 18,700 ha of
amorpha (Amorpha fruticosa), and 14,400 ha of desert willow (Salix microstachya).
Shrubs will be mainly planted on ridge tops, the upper part of north-ficing slopes, and



- 15 -
under harsh ecological conditions, such as those encountered on the high-altitude plateaus
of the Inner Mongolia project areas. Besides providing fuel, raw materials for particle
board and baskets (desert willow), bermes (seabuclthorn) and livestock feed (mainly
caragana), shrubs will be used to stabilize edges of gullies and highly erodible, steep
sloping areas. Shrub pasures planted under the project are expected to generate between
0.75 and 1.5 tons hay equivalent of relatively low quality forages per year, w:th the
highest yields being obtained from caragana and the lowest from desert willow.
3.8         Shrubs, such as seabuckthorn, together with black locust trees will also be
used for the planting of living hedges for subdivision of grazing areas. With the expected
large-scale grantng of exclusive user rights for grazing land to individual livestock owners,
living hedges will become of utmost importance and, therefore, their planting will be
encouraged and promoted under the project. Planting of shrub will be done from March
through November with seedlings to be provided by nurseries to be set up in each county.
New shrub plantations will be protected for the first three years after establishment. As
with pasture improvement, planting of shrub will be contracted out to farmers, who will
obtain exclusive, individual user rights for the land they plant.
3.9         Orchards are planned for about 27,000 ha, of which 20,000 ha will be fruit
orchards (mainly apple) and 7,000 ha will be planted to Chinese date and various nut
species. Excellent quality fruit can be produced in the project areas and, cunently,
domestic demand far exceeds production. The project wil expand the area under high-
yielding apple varieties such as 'Red Star and "Fuji," both of which have been
successfully introduced and perform well in the region. Seedling quality and extension
services have improved notably in recent years and sufficient experience has been
accumulated to further increase the orchard area on the Loess Plateau. However, although
growers in the project areas show notable expertise, there is a rneed to continue to identify
and adopt up-to-date orchard technology and to improve marketing strategies. The
expansion of orchards will also lead to an increasing demand for pest and disease control
solutions. The project will, therefore, provide for identifying, testing and disseminating
environmentally acceptable and cost-effective pest and disease control methods as part of
an integrated pest management (IPM) program (see para. 3.19).
3.10        Grasslands, to be established on 155,000 ha, will greatly increase the fodder
supply and hence increase the sustainable livestock carrying capacity of the project areas.
The main species would be alfalfa, astragalus, amaranth, and sweet clover. Alfalfa is
planted on low areas with good soil moisue, astragalus is planted on steeper slopes at
higher elevations and on the sandy areas. Productivity of grazing land has significantly
deteriorated during the past decade due to substantial build-up in livestock populations
(parficularly of sheep and goats) and the prevailing grazing practices under which
communally owned lands are gramzed by individually owned livestock. As in other
countries with similar grazng systems, such practices have led to severe overgrazing, soil
erosion and declines in herbage digestibility on virtually all project area pasures, as
livestock owners tend to graze as many animals as possible in an attempt to maximize their
share in profit from the common granng resource without reinvesting in pasture
improvement and maintenance.



- 16 -
3.11       Realization of sustainable livestock production systems and higher
productivity per livestock unit, while simultaneously reducing overgrazing and soil erosion,
is one of the objectives to be reached in the project areas. The project will increase the
avaiabity and quality of winter and spnng feed and will promote a more rational use of
natural grazing land and improved pastures. The main technical thmst of the proposed
forage and lvestock development component will be (a) increased production of forages,
both in terms of volume and quality, from improved pastures and forage shrubs; (b)
introduction of modem, intenasive forage crop production via adaptive research and tial
work on farmers' fields; and (c) dissemination of more rational utilization of grazing land
and improved pastures by fostering the granting of individual user rights to livestock
owners.
3.12        Sediment Control Dams. There are two types of dam; key dams and
warping dams. Key dams are located close to the outlet of subwatersheds with catchment
areas of 3-5 lkn2. The inital gross storage capacity of a key dam is generally between
0.5 million cubic meters (mcm) and 1 mcm, but it can be as high as 5 mcm. Warping
dams ( the term "warping' is explained below) are located on smaller tributary catchments
upstream of the key dams; their inital gross storage capacities are generally less than 0.5
mcm. Key dams serve several functions; sediment retention, flood control, and storage
of water for irrigation and village water supply. A key dam is normally built first on a
watershed as part of a development plan which provides for smaller warpng dams on
upstream tributaries soon after the completion of the key dam. The dams are homogenous
earth-fill embankments built in deeply incised gullies. The areas submerged by the dams
consist of eroded slopes and gully beds and are uninhabited. The project includes 236 key
dams and 2,208 warping dams. Of the latter, over 90 percet are small dams with a
storage capacity under 0.1 mcm, built in the gullies upstream and downstream of the large
dams. Their main purpose is to capture sediment flowing in from the sides of the gullies.
3.13        Design and construction standards for dams have been improved
considerably following some dam failures in 1977 when exceptionally intense storms hit
the Loess Plateau (see Amos 3, paras. 10-17 for details on design and safety procedures).
A notable feature of the smaller dams is that most of them are built without spillways. At
most sites it is cheaper to size the dam and reservoirs to store the entire volume of the
design flood than to provide a spillway. The geometry of the gullies is such that key dams
and warping dams have to be relatively high to serve their purpose; the range is usually
5-15 m but some are as high as 30 m. For most reservoirs, the bulk of the active storage
volume is in the top S m and, when sedimentation begins to infringe on the flood storage
capacity, the option is available at most sites to increase the height of the dam. This can
be done at a fraction of the initial cost since it involves a low embanknent placed on the
sediment behind the dam. In recent years a mode of construction using hydraulic fill has
been perfected. The loess material on the sides of the gully is sluiced with high pressure
hoses into the dam body. At many sites, raising could be carried out a number of times
since a damn typicaly occupies a small part of the total height of a gully. Each dam has
an outet conduit with an intale stucre designed to accommodate future sng of the
dam.



- 17 -
3.14        Warping land. aWarping,0 a term used to denote the formation of land
by the accumulation of river-borne sediment, is carried out on a large scale along the
Yellow River and its tributaries. It can consist of trapping silt in a reservoir or the
divers:on of river water into an offstream setting area. In the three watersheds in Inner
Mongolia, about 2,000 ha will be formed by diverting silt-laden river water to waste lands
along the rivers. Since the land is close to the rivers it will be irrigated. Elsewhere in the
project area, about 3,400 ha will be formed by sediment trapped by dams and gully control
works.
3.15        Irrigtion. Most of the irrigation is planned for Iner Mongolia, where
about 5,500 ha of land will be irrigated in small units of 20 to 30 ha along the three rivers
on lands reclaimed or protected by the project works. Shallow tubewells wiNl be the source
of water for most of them, but some will be supplied by pumping from the water
impounded by key and warping dams, or diversion dams. Much of the inigation will be
for orchards. A total of 900 ha will be irrigated in the other prvinces, mainly by
pumping from the reservoirs and here also the main users will be orchards.
3.16        Guly Control. In the project areas of Inner Mongolia and Gansu the gully
beds are narrow and lend themselves to control by check dams. These are low brush dams
built across the gullies at 20 to 30 m intervals. The effect is to slow the velocity of flow
during floods and thereby reduce undercutting of slopes which often leads to landslides.
Measures are also adopted, especially in the gulied plateau ladform, to arrest erosion at
the heads of gullies. Shrubs are planted along the edges of the gully heads, and small
dams are built in the gullies.
itutional Development
3.17        The organizational arangements for the project are described in Chapter 4.
The Upper and Middle Reach Bureau (UMRB), located in Xian, Shaauxi is the branch of
the Yellow River Conservancy Commission (YRCC) responsible for soil and water
conervation on the Loess Platu. The UMRB, and the project provinces, prefectures and
counties have demonstrated notable epertise in planning and implementing soil and water
conservation works. However, since the project is on a larger scale than previous
underakings, these agencies see a need to enhance their capabilities in planning, design,
project management and monitoring, with particular emphasis on improved management
iinformation systems (Annex 5). Improvements in these areas will have long-term benefits
in view of the large areas to be developed in the Loess Plateau. This component of the
project will strengthen the UMRB's capacity as a leading agency for soil and water
conservation in the Yellow River Basin, and the provinces and prefectures will be better
equipped to meet the formidable challenge of developing the Loess Plateau. Also, since
the project will be implemented largely by the villagers living in the watersheds, thxre will
be a need for farmer training on a much larger scale than in earlier projects. Effective and
timely training will call for new approaches and new facilities at the county and prefecture
levels. The institutional development component will finance the following items:



- 18 -
(a) construction and equipping of training centers at the provincial, prefecture
and county levels;
(b)   vehicles and equipment for extension services;
(c) hardware, software and related training for geographic information systems
(GIS), computer aided design (CAD), and project information systems
mnanagement;
(d) local and overseas training and study tours; and
(e)   rearch and the services of extera ers for Technical Panels.
3.18        Trning. Training centers will be established at Project Management
Offices (PMOs) at the provincial, prefectwe and county levels. Senior officials will be
trained in project planning and management, including computer based management
formation systems. Technical staff will receive additional training in improved methods
Of watershed planning and design, including aids to planning and monitoring such as GIS
and CAD. At the township and village level, training of village chiefs, farmer technicians
and farmers will focus on the construction of soil and water conservation works and the
management of the newly created farmland, forests and grsands. Some 22 subject areas
of training have been identified. All personnel and farmers engaged in the project will
attend courses to fmiliariz them with the technical standards. During the life of the
prqect it is estmated that 260 senior officials, 2,240 middle level managers and key
wtnicians, and 131,000 farme technicians and farners will receive training. Overseas
trang, consitng of shor tmning courses and study tours will be provided for selected
speiaists and managers. Some 10 topics, relevant to project development, have so far
been identified, involving missions to 12 countries. Overseas taining will total about 260
staff-months. For the most part, instuctors will be drawn from managers with
considerale expeence in soil and water consevation, and from technical staff of the
special bureaus, but outside specialists will be employed, where necessary, to introduce
new technology. At negotiations, assanc  were obtained t at taning to be conducted
under the nstudonal Development Component vwil be caried ou in accordance with
programs agreed with MA. To ta end, each project province shal prepare, and submt
to the CPMO, forfojarding to die Association by November 30 of each year, a proposed
program of training acdvides to be carred out during the folowing calendar year.
3.19       Research.  The project's research progamm is aimed at refining and
improving the wide range of land development techniques that are currently being applied
in the Loess Plateau. Most of the work will be carried out by existing research institutions
and exerimental stations under contract to the PPMOs. Ongoing research activities will
be supported and, where necessary, expanded to serve the project. The following main
topics will be covered:
(a)   crop protection and integrated pest management ()   techniques for
orchards;



- 19 -
(b)  fertility enhancement through growth hormones, nitrogen fixing rhizobium,
and mycorrhza;
(c)   green manuring and composting;
(d)   forestry seedling production and plantation establishment;
(e) orchard management;
(f)   grssland and forage establishment and management;
(g)  livestock management, livestock control, and pen feeding of stock;
(h)   shrub management, vegetative contour barriers and live hedge development;
0)   terrace and dam construction and maintenance; and
(j)   socioeconomic aspects of watershed development.
3.20        In each province, the research program will be managed and coodinated by
Provincial technical panels composed of researchers from existing institutions and saei
project officials. Field trials will be conducted in the project areas so that successful trials
can be incorporated in demonstration plots. The program will include exchange progrms
with researchers in other provinces and overseas research institutions (Annx 4).
3.21        Technology Transfer. The transfer of technology within the project, and
from outside of the project, will improve its implementation and enhance its benefits. In
preparing the project, the planners and designers have drawn on the skiBls and techniques
that they and the farmers have accumulated in recent large-scale land development
schemes. The experience with these schemes has revealed a considerable potential for
further innovation, refinement of techniques, and the introduction of new technology. In
the area of project management, geographic informiation systems have applis to
project planning and subsequent monitoring; computer aided design has applications to
detailed design; and wider use of desk-top computers can permit more effective project
scheduling and accounting. As regards afforestation, orchards and gasslands a number
of promising oppoftunities for innovations and new cultural practices have emerged during
the pation of the project. And, although the existg agiculturl and livestock
services provide timely and effective advice to farmers in crop and livestock production,
the improvements in soil and water conseation provided by the project will provide new
opporties and challenges. The research program described above will be an important
source of new technolegy. The project's training program will be designed to effect the
transfer of technology. The Central Project Management Office (CPMO) and the PMOs
at the provincial, prefecure and county levels will be responsible for managing technology
transfer. In this they will be asisted by the technical panels.

C. PLANNING AND DESIGN
3.22        There are three stages m the watershed rehabilitation plang and design
process. Land use and land capability surveys have been carried out for each watershed
and subwatrshed. The vilage leaders work closely with county and township agricultural
planners in the designs and their implementation. Those surveys then form a basis for the
stage plan of the project which defines the future land use pattem and specifies the crops,
shrubs and trees to be grown. This has been completed for the entire project area. The
second stage is detailed planning on a subwatershed level which is based on 1:10,000
maps. Detailed design is based on technical standards and on larger scale maps depending
on the type of land development. In April 1994, detailed plans were available for one
third (over 300) of the subwatersheds. Detailed plans specify the main category of land
use for units of less than one ha, and provide further specifications within each category.
For example, detailed designs for an area selected for afforestation specify the types of
trees and shrubs depending on slope, soils, aspect and other relevant factors. For
teracing, detailed designs give all the information needed to construct the terraces to the
latest standards which apply throughout the project area.
3.23        Key dams are designed by the provincial water resource bureaus according
to standards developed by the Ministry of Water Resources in 1986 ("Technical Standard
for Major Gully Control Structures," SD175-86). These standards have been endorsed by
the Planning Academy of MWR as well as the Ministry of Energy. These specfy all
aspects of design and construction including planning procedures, site selection
requirements, dam body layout, calculation of hydrologic design parameters, foundation
design, choice of construction mateials, construction techniques, hydrologic and hydraulic
design. These standards have been reviewed by IDA engineers and found acceptable.
3.24        The current procedures require multilevel technical reviews and approval of
the design and construction of sediment control dams. Preliminary designs are carred out
by county water and soil conservation bureaus. Initial review and approval of struct
design and siting is carred out by provincial water and soil conservation authorities who
in turn, submit construction applications to the UMRB for final independent review and
approval. The ultimate responsibility for ensuring compliance with design procedures and
technical specifications rests with the UMRB. Dunng negotiations, assurances were
obtained that al dams (a) which will either exceed 10 m in height or create a reservoir
with a gross storage volume of more than 2,500,000 cubic meters or (b) which possess
unusual design features, involve difficult geological conditons, or are situated so as to be
signfcanty destructive in the event of a failure, wil be ri) designed and constucted
according to standards and procedures acceptable to IDA (including independent reviws
of the designs and construction) and (it) periodically inspected in accordance with sound
engineering practices in order to determine whether there are arn deficiencies whkh may
endanger their sqfey.
3.25        In addition to the dams, detailed standards for design and construction of the
other soil and water conservation works have been prepared by the UMRB and the



- 21 -
provinces. These technical standards have been issued to all entities participating in the
project and will be the standards adopted for quality control.
D. IMP nTAIION SCEDULE
3.26        Ihe project will be implemented over a period of eight years beginning in
January 1994. Project activities would be completed by December 31, 2001, and the
closing date would be December 31, 2002. However, work has been under way since
October 1993 to establish and equip the project management offices at the county level,
and to expand the facilities and staff at the existing provincial and prefecture project
offices. Much of the construction will be by manual labor. The construction season
depends on the climate and the farmers' cropping cycle. Work can be carried out for four
to five weeks in March/April after the ground has thawed, for four weeks after the wheat
harvest in July/August, and for six to eight weeks in October/November. Thus, for
manual labor the construction season lasts for about 120 days. For equipment, there are
about 150 days in a year when construction can proceed. Construction of preliminary
works began in September 1993. Implementation schedules are shown in Annex 5, Table
1.
E. Cosr ESTMATS
3.27        Cost estimates are based on experience with similar works in the provinces.
The costs for the land development components are derived by applying per ha costs to the
total areas to be developed. For each type of land development the unit costs per ha are
derived from recent field data on inputs of labor, materials and, where appropriate,
construcion equipment. For dam construction, costs are derived in a similar way with unit
prices per cubic meter being calculated for earthwork and concrete. A daily rate of Y 6
for unskilled labor and Y 8 for skilled labor has been used for the estimates, and the recent
field data confirm that this is a reasonable rate. Opportunities for off-farm employment
for people in the project areas are limited, and are mostly available to the younger males
who are prepared to live away from home. For example, when farmers choose to pay
their land lease payments by contributing labor, the imputed daily rate is below Y 6 and
can be as low as Y 4.
3.28       The base cost is in end-1993 prices.  Since there are no significant
unaccounted physical factors, a physical contingency of 5 percent has been applied to the
total project cost. Labor rates for off-season employment on the project works are not
likely to be subject to the sharp escalation now being experienced in more prosperous parts
of China. Also, many of the villagers employed on the project will stand to bnefit from
the improvements in land productivity and, therefore, the counties will have some leverge
in setting rates. Taking these factors into account, the price contingencies adopted by the
mission are based on the lower of three sets of escalation factors proposed by the Bank for
projects in China. Thus, expected price increases for the total project cost expressed in
Yuan were derived by applying price escalation of 12.0 percent for 1994, 9 percent for
1995, 8 percent for 1996, and 6.0 percent for the following years. For the cost expressed
in US dollars the escalation factors are 1.2 percent for 1994, 2.4 percent for 1995, and



- 22 -
3.4 percent after 1995. Cost estimates are summarized in Table 3.1 and details are given
in Annex 6

G. PRoCUiEMET
3.30         The project works consist of various forms of land development and will be
caried out on thousands of small plots of land in the vicinity of about 2,100 villages.
Terracing and irrigation ($101.6 million), afforestation ($65.1 million), and grasslands
($13.6 million) will be performed on the basis of force account or direct negotiations by



- 23 -
labor drawn from the nearby villages under the leadership of the county and township
technical staff. This approach is consistent with the strong tradition in China of public
participation at all stages of a project's implementation. In many cases, the villagers will
lease the land they have helped to develop and be subsequently responsible for cost
recovery. The construction of the key dams, estimated to cost $250,000 equivalent or
more, will be procured ($0.5 million) through LCB. Other works for key dams, warping
dams and other small structures such as gully head protection works and ponds ($20.6
million) will be carried out by county and township construction teams, or private
construction teams formed by the villagers on the basis of force account or direct
negotiation. Most sites are accessible only to small construction equipment such as the
local 75 hp bulldozers, and most of the dams are built using a type of hydraulic fill
construction suited to the loess soil. The irrigation component consists of a variety of
small projects involving tubewells, pumping from water stored by the dams, and river
diversions. These will be constructed by county teams or private groups contracted by the
counties.
3.31        Agricultural inputs ($9 million), vehicles ($4 million), and reinforcing steel
($3.7 million), will be procured through International Competitive Bidding in accordance
with Bank Group Guidelines. Contracts for these items shall be grouped into bid packages
estimated to cost the equivalent of $250,000 or more and bidders for supply of ferilizer
shall be prequalified. Qualified domestic bidders competing under ICB will be eligible for
a 15 percent preference in bid evaluation, or the customs duties and import tax payable by
a nonexempt importer, whichever is the least. Equipment and materials for training
centers, seedling nurseries, and laboratories ($2 rflfion), which are estimated to cost
$250,000 equivalent or less per contract, will be procured through LIB or local and
international shopping. Contracts for works for buildings for trai.ing centers,
demonstration farms, etc. ($5.1 million), will be procured through LCB procedures
acceptable to IDA. Contracts for research and local and foreign training will be awarded
following comparison of technical proposals, and consultants and specialists for the
technical panels and training will be employed in accordanice with Bank Group Guidelines
for the Employment of Consultants ($7.6 million).
3.32        IDA Review. Prequalification and bid documents, bid evaluations, and
award recommendations will be subject to IDA prior review for goods contracts with an
estimated cost of $250,000 or more and works contracts of $500,000 or more, and ail
contracts for consultants services. This represents about 80 percent of the IDA-financed
contracts for goods and 30 percent of the IDA-financed contracts for works. The
procurement profile is shown in Table 3.2, and a procurement table is attached in Annex
2. The Intemational Tendering Company (rrC) of the China Technical Import and Export
Coopeation has been chosen to represent the borrower in procurement matters.

3.34        Retroactive fmancing has been recommended for expenditures for land
development and locally procured goods incurred between November 15, 1993 and the date
of signing of the Credit Agreement. The construction season in the project areas depends
on the climate and the farmers' cropping cycle. The provinces began construction in
winter of 1993 to ensure a full working season. Retroactive financing would also cover
the necessary funds for completion of detailed designs for one tiird or about 300 of the
small watersheds. The total disbursable amount will be SDR 7.0 million ($10.0 million
equivalent) based on work programs reviewed and agreed by IDA.
3.35        Withdrawal applications for land development works would be supported by
progress reports showing actual physical quantities and unit prices, the latter to be updated
annually and agreed by IDA. The land development components account for 81 percent
of the total cost. Each major form of land development (terraces, trees, shrubs,
grasslands) will be assigned an average unit price per hectare. IDA will disburse a
percentage of the expendiures in a given period which will be calculated as the product
of the area completed multiplied by the unit price. For dams, the disbursement will be
against a unit price per cubic meter of earthwork. These unit rates will be updated each
year on the basis of actuaI average costs realized during the previous year. Sutaments of
expenditures (SOEs) will be required for disbursement to be made against (a) contracts for
goods costing $250,000 equivalent or less, (b) training and study tours, and (c) force
account.
3.36        To facilitate disbursement, a Special Account to be operated by MOF will
be established in a bank acceptable to IDA. The Special Account will be set up in an
amount of $10 million, the estimated scale of disbursement over a four-month period.

J. ENVIRONMENTAL AND SoL IMPACLS
3.38        The prqect will lead to significant changes in land use (AmexZ). Large
areas of waste and unused land carrying poor quality grass, shrubs and trees will be
planted to more suitable and productive varieties. The overall effects of these land use
changes will be to pzeserve the land resource, and increase productivity in an
environmtally susinable manner. The land under forest and shrubs will incrase from
210,000 ha to 510,000 ha. Of the area of 430,000 ha presently devoted to field crops,
about 110,000 ha of the more steeply sloping land will be converted to forests. The
remaining 320,000 ha under cros will be on higher-yielding lands which will be teced
to inproved standards. There will be a small increase in the area of grassland, but much
of the existing grassland will be replanted to grasses with higher livestock carryig
capacities, and will be managed by individual farmers or farmer groups. The key dams
and warping dams will intercept virtually aU of the sediment from the areas they control,
and this will have significant downstream benefits; the dams wil eliminate destuctive flash
floods, provide water for irigation and other uses, and the sediment deposited upstream
of the dams will form high quality farm land.
3.39        The project has been planned and designed in close consultaton with those
villages with the right to cultivate the land being developed. The increased productivity
is esimated to more than double farm incomes at full development. Thus, the project has
a ignificant impact in terms of poverty alleviation. There is no relocation of population.
3.40        The sediment control dams will be sited in uninhabited gullies and,
therefore, no relocation of population wiUl be necessary in the submergence areas. The
topography of the watersheds allows considerable flexibility in siting the dams, and all will
be located at sites remote from existng or potential setdlements. Design and construction
procedures for the dams are consvative, but in the unlikely event of failure there would
be no danger to life and limited damage to property (see Annex 3.
3.41        There are no natal forests or wetlands in the project areas, and thus
wildlife resources are meager. The project will, however, increase forest cover from
14 percent to 38 percent, and evidence from recently developed areas suggests that this will



- 27 -
lead to a higher and more diverse population of birds and smal mammals. The higher
standards of crop management will not lead to any increase in the rimoff of ferdlizers
(herbicides and insecticides are seldom used in field crops) since most of the crop land will
be teraced. The expansion of orchards will lead to some increase in pesficide use, but an
objective of the project is to disseminate techniques for safer and more efficient use of
chemical controls. Assurances were obtained at negotiations that allpesdcides used under
the project shall have been approved by the Association, and that the storage, hanling,
distribuion and use of such pestcides shallfollow guidelines acceptable to IDA. These
are the respective FAO Code of Conduct on the Distribution and Use of Pesticides, and
FAO Guidelines for the Packaging and Storage of Pesticides.
3.42        Environmental Impact Assessments (ETAs) have been prepared by each
province and these form the basis for a Summary EIA for the project prepared by the
UMRB under the guidance of scientists from the Yellow River Conservancy Commission.
This high quality report, prepared in accordance with the Bank Group's recommended
format and guidelines for ElAs, finds that the project's environmental and social impacts
are wholly beneficial and there are no adverse impacts, and contains detailed proposals for
environmental management and monitoring. The report was approved by the National
Environmental Protection Agency (NEPA) and was sent to IDA prior to appraisal.
Assurances were obtained at negotatons that each project province shall  plement the
part of the Environmental Monitoring and Management Plan applicable to Its respectve.
part of the project in a manner satsfactory to the Associaon.
K. TECNCAL ASSIrANCE
3.43        Assistance from  nonproject agencies, such as Chinese Academy of
Agricultural Sciences (CAAS), and consultants within China is required and is included
under the research, technology transfer, and training components. Areas where foreign
TA would be of value would be in forestry and orchard nursery and field management, in
new computer applications-GIS in particular, and in pasture and forage development.
One of the best ways this could be provided would be through participation, by proven and
experienced specialists, on the project's technical panels. The specialists would be
expected to provide a medium-term commitment to the project and would provide an
independent view of project investments and technology, as well as providing specific
recommendations in their area of specaliztion. These specialists would be funded by the
project or through bilateral agreements with donor agencies. Bilawtal agencies might
prove useful for forest nursery and orchard management, Integrated Pest Management
(IPM) technology, forage development, livestock, and forest nursery management, soil and
water conservation practices and for soil fertlty maintenance, and for GIS. There are also
a number of university consorda that would be interested in expanding their ties with the
project in areas of research. IDA staff would help the project organize some of these
bilateral arrangements during project implementation.
