4. PROJECT ORGANIZAIION AND MANAGEMENT
A. PROJEcr ORGANIATION
4.1         The Ministry of Water Resources (MWR) includes in its jurisdiction
stewardship of the land and water resources of China's major river basins. MWR
exercises this responsibility through seven river basin conservancy commissions. The
Yellow River Conservancy Commission (YRCC), based in Zhengzhou, Henan Province,
and its Upper and Middle Reach Bureau (JMRB), based 500 kn upstream at Xian,
Shaanxi Province, are the lead agencies for the proposed project. The UMRB and the
governments of Shanxi, Inner Mongolia, Shaanxi and Gansu provinces would be
responsible for the overall project implementation under the general supervision of YRCC.
YRCC is the principal administrative, advisory and coordinating agency within the Yellow
River Basin. It manages centrally financed or joint central-provincial water resource
construction works and soil and water conservation programs within the basin. UMRB is
the leading agency responsible for soil and water conservation activities in the upper and
middle reaches (more than 90 percent of the basin area) of the Yellow River. Since 1986
it has planned and supervised watershed management on an area of 60,000 knm2 covering
four major tributaries of the Yellow River, including 1.3 million ha of afforestation and
the establishment of 60 Monitoring and Evaluation (M&E) stations to monitor the impact
of erosion control measures. About 500 key control dams were constructed under their
supervision and several thousand more are planned to be built in an area of 270,000 mh'
during the next 10 years. The management structure would include Project Leading
Groups (PLGs) and Project Management Offices (PMOs) at the provincial, prefecture,
county and township levels. (A project organization chart is attached in AnneA 8.) In
addition, Technical Panels (TPs) have also been set up at central and provincial levels
(para. 4.5).
4.2         Project Leading Groups. Project Leading Groups (PLGs) were established
at the central, provincial, prefecture, county and township levels and have played an
instrumental role during project preparation. The central PLG is headed by a vice minister
of MWR and composed of one vice governor from each of the four project provinces,
several directors of YRCC, MWR's Bureau of Foreign Affairs, Planning, Water and Soil
Conservation, Water Resources, Finance and four provincial PMOs which formulate
project-related policies and plans, provide guidance for project overall design, review and
resolve major issues related to the project, and coordinate efforts among the four project
provinces and other govemnment agencies. Provincial PLGs are headed by a vice governor
in charge of agriculture and assisted by representatives from the Planning Commission,
Departments of Finance, Agriculture, Water Conservancy, Forestry, Water and Soil
Conservation Bureau and the Agncultural Bank of China. The main responsibilities of the
provincial PLG are, within their own jurisdiction, to work out detailed measures for



- 29 -
implementing the policies and plans formulated by the central PLG, approve project
feasibility study, organize counterpart funds, decide the distribution and repayment of
proceeds of the IDA credit, and enlist support of other relevant agencies. The Provincial
PLGs will meet as needed to review progress, coordinate the activities of other agencies,
approve annual work programs and budget, and provide guidance and supervision to the
Provincial-level PMOs. Provincial PLGs will also keep the provincial governments
apprised of the overall progress of implementation. PLGs at prefecture, county and
township levels will have a similar structure and responsibilities.
4.3         Central Project Office. A Central Project Management Office (CPMO) has
been established at UMRB headquarters in Xian to provide technical oversight and
interprovincial coordination during project implementation. Its main responsibilities
include the overall supervision of the project activities in the provinces; preparing progress
reports including the project completon report; arranging combined procurement under
ICB and major LCB; coordinating institutional development aspects, particularly overseas
training, study tours and techimcal assistance; reviewing the provincial applications for
withdrawal of the proceeds of the IDA credit; keeping close contact with central
govemment agencies; and liaising with IDA on behalf of project provinces.
4.4         Project Management Offic". Provincial PMOs have been established at
the Water Conservancy Departments. They are responsible for project implementation
while coordinating activities and inputs of other technical departnents (bureaus).
Provincial PMOs would (a) draw up annual, detailed project work programs and budgets;
(b) review and approve lower level PMO designs and work plans; (c) undertake
procurement not covered by the central project office and distribute procured materials;
(d) prepare and submit to the cental PMO detailed project accounts, audits and progress
rorts; (e) undertake project monitoring of physical and financial progress and evaluations
of project impact; and (f) organize research, local training, and project supervision. PMOs
at lower levels have been established under the Water Conservancy (Water and Soil
Conservation) Bureaus with a similar structure and responsibilities. All these PMOs have
been involved in the dtiled preparation of the project and will be responsible for all
aspects of implementation. The township PMOs have been worldng very closely with the
villagers through vilLage committees in developing project proposals. They will continue
to play a key role in project implementation through organizing the collecve effort at the
village level which is crucial to the success of the project. Assurances were obtained at
negotiations dtat CPMO and PMOs at provincial, prefecture, county and township leveks
would be maintained with fuwtions and responsibilities acceptable to IDA and staffed by
competent staff in adequate nwnbers.
4.5         Technical Panels (rPs) have been established at the CPMO in the UMRB
and the Provincial PMOs. The Central Technical Panel (CTP) includes the leading
specialists in the UMRB, technical representatives from each province, representatives
from the CPMO, and several external specialists. CTP will also retain two or three
foreign consultants on short-term assignments as members. The foreign consultants will
provide special expertise in such areas as soil fertility, forage development, and integrated
pest management (1PM). The main tasks of the CTP include periodic review of project



- 30 -
technologies. These reviews will (a) recommend revisions to the Technical Standards, (b)
review and comment on annual research and training plans, (c) identify innovative
techniques for consideration by the provinces, (d) review the effectiveness of technology
dissemination practices, and (e) generally act as a sounding board for the advancement of
new techniques. The CTP will advise the CPMO on the need for obtaining technical
expertise from outside the project for new techniques or to address specific problems; it
will also advise on overseas training. The Provincial Technical Panel (PIP) will consist
of leading specialists from the technical bureaus and some external specialists. The PTP
will prepare an annual report on technology issues and research for consideration by the
provincial PMO and any special issues will be referred to the CTP. An assurance was
obtained at negotiations that the provinces and the CPMO will maintain Technical Panels
with memberships, functions and responsibilities acceptable to IDA, and described above.
B. FINANCIAL MANAGEMENI
4.6         Flow of Funds. MOF would make available the proceeds of the IDA credit
to the governments of the four project provinces at an annual interest rate of 3 percent with
a repayment period of 20 years, including an 8-year grace period. The foreign exchange
risk will be borne by them. A portion of the IDA funds will be used directly by CPMO
and the provinces to cover the costs of research, technical assistance, training and
equipment expenses. The provincial financial departments, representing the provincial
governments, will channel the remaining funds successively to prefecture, county and
township governments. Funds would be made available to villages and farmers only for
activities that have been appraised and approved by the PMOs. According to Chinese
pcice, contracts will be signed at each level of government and at each stage of
relending operation, indicating the terms of lending and repayment obligations.
4.7         Domestic funds, which account for about 40 percent of project costs, will
be contributed by government grants at central, provincial, prefecture and county levels,
and by project beneficiaries who will contribute in cash or in kind or both. According to
Chinese practice, terms and conditions for use of government grants will also be indicated
in contracts signed at each level of government and between project beneficiaries and
relevant government agencies.
4.8         Cost Recovery. Full recovery of the IDA portion of total project investnent
costs would be guaranteed by the Finance Departments of the participating provinces. A
detailed repayment plan has been established based on investment cost for each productivity
increase and timing of the revenue stream. Under the proposed repayment plan, the IDA
credit would be recovered from the beneficiaries and the local governments (for the portion
used for research, training and technical assistance). Combined with the farmers'
contribution of about 13 percent, more than two thirds of the total project cost would be
recovered.-/ In conformity with contracts indicating the principles, terms, instruments
jf    Users of newly developed uigation facilities will repay about 50 percent of the investmt coast
through their household responsibility contracts and will, in addition, pay water charges uffici.nt
to cover O&M costs.

C. LAND ComrRAcrs AND MANAGEMENr
4.9         The reforms of the late 1970s and early 1980s introduced the household
responsibility system to China's fanners. Under this system, a household is given use of
and responsibility for specific plots of land in contrast to the former practice of collective
farming. The introduction of this system is considered key to China's successful rural
reform program which, together with other reforms, has led to the dramatic growth in
China's agricultural production over the past decade. In the Loess Plateau area, the land
contract system has been extended beyond crop land to the areas reclaimed by
afforestation, grasslands and even wastelands. In the areas to be developed under the
project, farmer households or groups of households would be allotted land under long-term
contra  which will specify management standards. In return, the farmers will have the
right to fully benefit from the output of the land for which they are responsible.
4.10        All land developed under the project would be contraced out to farmer
households or household groups. New land contracts would be signed between farmers
and local governments to explicitly state the terms, land use right and obligations under
which farmers' interests would be legally protected. All farmers aff:4ted by the project
would receive properly signed, sealed and registered contracts, copies of such contrs
would be held in the township or county registries and would be available for inspection.
The success of the project will depend on famers having adequate incentives to realize the
benefits of the land development. These incentives would in turn depend on the farmers
having confidence in their land contracts. The current terms for land contract vary
between 10 years and 30 years and are frequently adjusted. This insecurity of land tenure
is the single most important disincentive for any long-term improvement to the land.
Under the proposed project, longer terms for land contract would be applied in all areas
covered by the project, i.e., a minimum of 50 years for steep and waste land; 50 years for
forestry plots; 30 years for orchards, terraces and warping land. An assurance was
obtained at negotliaons that the project provinces woud cause exclusive land use rights
for terms between 30 years and 50 years to be granted to participating households.
4.11        Most planing, implementation and supervision of land development would
be the prmary responsibility of county and township PMOs, and would be reviewed by
prefecture PMOs and approved by provincial PMOs under the overall coordination of
CPMO. Land development would be mostly carried out by the farmers in the project
areas. Construction works would be undertaken by work teams, local farmers or
conacted out to construction teams depending on the complexity of worls and local



- 32 -
circumstances. An assurance was obtained at negotiations that the provincial PMOs would
prepare and submit, through CPMO, a detailed annual budget and work plan to IDA for
review by November 30 of each year in respect ofproject inplementation in the following
year, and carry out such plans, as approved by the Association.
D. MONmR1NG, EVALUAIION, A   REPORTING
4.12        The primary objecdves of the project are to conserve soil and moisture,
reduce sediment flow transportation to downstream areas, and improve farm incomes
through land improvement investments and improved farm practices. AU of these aspects
would be carefully moitored. The project will apply a strategy of comprehensive
development to 812 subwatersheds located in four provinces and in 21 counties and
municipalities. Within each subwatershed a senes of activities will take place over a
period of 3 to 5 years. Thorough monitoring will be essential for upgrding subwatershed
design during implementation. Also monitoring of the execution of physical investments
is essential to enable IDA to reimburse against completed activities that meet the quality
standards laid out in the Project's Technical Standards Manual.
4.13        Monitoring.   Monitoring of activities at all levels-especially at
subwatershed level-will be supported by appropriate computer applications, currently
identified as the computeized project management software, and an accounting package.
It is proposed to link these applications to a GIS data base system (such as ATLAS-GIS)
that will allow a spatial identification of project activities, as well as provide for the
collection and display of social and economic data. Details of the proposed Management
Information System and monitoring indicators are provided in Annex 5.
4.14        Project management software (such as MS Project) will be used for project
planning and scheduling. It will be used at four levels-Upper and Middle Reaches Bureau
(l)-CPMO, Provincial PMO (4)-PPMO, Prefectue PMO (optional), and the County
PMO (21). Tables in Annex S demonstrate typical reporting formats that will be used for
each subwatershed. They include job site/contractor codes, unit of investment, and
planned and scheduled costs, start and finish dates. The annex also demonstrates how MS
Project can be used for planning and monitoring at higher management levels and how it
can be linked to accounting software and the GIS data base.
4.15        At the county PMO, a separate file will be created for each subwatershed
and, together with other subwatershed files, aggregated to the UMRB level. Thus, the
project wi}l at all levels keep track of investment and development activities. This data
when linked to a GIS-generated data base will effectively deal with the tracking of inputs
and financial inflows and outflows. The GIS da!a base will provide the spatal link
between costs and inputs, and results.
4.16        Monitoring Sediment and Environmental Changes and Benefits. All four
provinces will monitor changes in water quality, sediment tansport reduction, on-site
erson, precipitation, and cages in soil fertlity. The project will provide additional
instrmentation to allow effective measurement and collection of data; some automatic



- 33 -
measuring devices will be installed m remote areas. Hard data fom collecting stations will
be reinforced by the use of satellite imagery and mobile field teams that will undertake
special investigations at selected sites. All the data will be entered into the Project's GIS
data system.
4.17        The UMRB, and the Provincial and County Soil and Water Conservation
Bureaus are experienced in the data collection envisaged under the projecL Data will be
collected on a continuing basis and every third year a full analysis will be undertaken to
track changes that are occuring. Selected subwatersheds will receive special attention in
order to identify early trends in environmental changes. Past monitoring of subwatersheds
under comprehensive treatment has provided the basis for developing the expected benefits
of this project, and they are substantial. The project's monitoring system will continue the
process, but on a wider scale; and will assure regular feedback to the Provincial and
UMRB technical panels for consideration in the review of research and technical standards.
The project will also assess the impact of nonproject road constrution, mining, and
industa and civil construction as point sources for erosion and sediment, and their impact
on overall sediment flows in the project areas.
4.18        Social and Economic Monitoring. The project will depend on two sources
of data to determiine social and economic changes. First it will use data that are collected
annually by the statistical authorities on economic changes. Secondly it will undertake
reresentative village surveys and field investigations to measure the impact with and
without the project. Special attention will be given to measuring changes in output due to
investments in land development and to the new technologies introduced by the project.
At the same time household investigations will measure the impact of the project on
increased household wealth, and improved access to social services. These data will be
reained in the project's GIS data base thus allowing close corrlation between investments,
their quality and cost, and social and economic benefits. Because investments will be
identified in detail (i.e., it wil be possible to define precisely where each site is located,
the costs incurred, the management practices applied, and annual yield data), it wil be
reaively easy to undertake analysis of different sites and extrapolate that data to the
watershed as a whole. This information would then support household and other surveys.
4.19        Organization and Costs.  CPMO wiU have overall responsibility in
ensuning that the provinces cary out effective monitonng and that standardized
methodologies are used. CPMO will prepare annually a monitoring report for the overaU

county levels. Where necessary PPMOs will contract other agencies and institutions to
undertake special studies, particularly of sociological and economic impacts. Each year
the Monitonng Plan and feedback will be reviewed by the technical committees and panels.

5. PRICES, PRODUCTION, AND MARKETS
A. PRICES
5.1         China's agricultural pnce reforms over the past decade have adjusted
procurement pnces of goods marketed through state channels, reduced the number of
goois subject to state monopoly purchase, freed many agricultural commodities from all
state price control, and increased the volumes of commodities under the state control which
are marketed at near-market prices. In 1993, only 10 percent of retail value of agricultural
commodities and 15 percent of retail value of agricultural raw mateials traded at official
state prices. The economy is now more market oriented, allowing producers to respond
to changes in market demand.
5.2         State procurement prices for grain almost doubled from 1988 to 1993 in line
vith national policies. These increases have brought the procurement prices of wheat,
maize, and oilseeds close to world prices. On April 1, 1993, Shaanxi, Shand, Inner
Mongolia, and Gansu provinces announced the full liberalization of grain markets. Prices
are now set at or near free market prices, and all direct government subsidies have been
eliminated. After harvest the wheat price in the project region is about Y 0.75/kg whether
sold to the free market or to the government.
5.3        The average market price in the region for apples in 1993 was Y 1.8-2.0/kg.
This represents an average price across all varieties and qualities produced in the area.
Apple prices have been fairly stable during the last five years, but showed a slight
downward trend in 1993. Apple quality ranks highest in China in Shaamxi province, and
usualy a prenium price above Y 2/kg can be fetched even at local markets. However,
due to the rapid production increase in the area, price declines are likely to occur over the
next few years. Therefore, for analytical purposes, a more conservative price of Y 1.1-1.3
per kg has been adopted. This is felt to be a more realistic sustainable market price.
Tobacco is the only commodity under the project which is still subject to state contract
purchase at prices announced annually by the provincial government, with farmers required
to produce according to production quotas.
5.4        In recent years input prices have outpaced output prices. In the future,
farmers wil have to procure al inputs at market prices. These prices are 15-20 percent
higher than the formerly subsidized prices. However, ceiling prices will be fixed annually
by public authorties to avoid speclation. The project counties have seed companies
which produce or procure certified seeds of suitable varieties of the main crops at
affordable cost. They have marketing and distribution facilities which also seU vegetable
seeds and agrohemicals, and opeAte seed cleaning facilities. Seed is grwn under
contract by smallholders, field inspected and certified to national standards. Increaing



- 36 -
demand could be dealt with by existing facilities through expansion of the contract system.
Incremental requirements of other major inputs can be handled by existing distribution
facilities.
5.5        Input and output prices used in crop budgets for financial analysis of the
project components are either average 1993 market prices or end- 1993 prices and are based
on data provided by the project authorties. These pnces are summarized in AnnexZ,
Tables 1 and 2. For economic analysis, World Bank commodity price projections have
been used to calculate the future farmgate values of wheat, maize, beef, urea, rock
phosphorus, and double ammonium phosphate (DAP). Detailed calculations are shown in
Annex 7, Table 3.

5.7        Crops. Grain production dominates the cropping system with one or two
harvests per year or three harvests in two years. The main crops are millet, corn,
buckwheat, potato, and beans. The total area sown to grain is 350,000 ha with a total
production of 430,000 tons. The sown area of millet is 240,000 ha (68 percent), potatoes
are planted on 63,000 ha, the bean area is 50,000 ha. Important oil crops are rapeseed,
linseed, sunflower, peanut, and castor. The total area is 55,000 ha with 35,000 tons of
annual production. The main cash crops are melons, vegetables, and herbs coverng an
area of 23,000 ha and a total output of 220,000 tons.
5.8        Under the project, crop output will incrse substantially through
significantly higher crop yields. More than 100,000 ha of low yielding and unstable crop
land on slopes will be taken out of production and converted into perennial cover crop.



- 37 -
At the same time, more than 83,200 ha of slope land will be converted into high yielding
terraces.
5.9         The total output of grain and fruit will increase from 427,000 tons and
80,000 tons to 660,000 and 320,000 tons, respectively. Per capita grain output and net
incomes will increase from 367 kg and Y 288 to 507 kg and Y 770. This will be achieved
despite the significant reduction in the planted area as a result of improved water retention
and improved cropping patterns. Yields are generally 200 to 250 percent higher on
terrces as compared to slopeland even at the same input level; and typically more stable
during periods of drought. Further yield increases are readily achieved through
intensification such as increased ferilizer application and the use of higher yielding crops
and varieties. At present, the cropping pattern is severely limited on the slopes, as about
60 percent of the area is sown with millet Production of the much higher yielding and
more profitable crops such as com, sunflower and castor is resticted to leveled land.
Yields of the major crops are expected to increase as summarnzd in Annex 7.
5.10        Production on the newly created warping land is fully incremental, as the
gully bottoms are generally wasteland before warping. Yields on land created behind silt
retention structures are on average about five to six times higher (without irrigaton) than
on slope land and with irrigation eight to ten times the slopeland level. These yield
increases are consistently realized in the project area and are considered achievable under
the project.
5.11        Forestry. The project will help alleviate a serious shortage of lumber,
poles, fuel wood, and fodder in the area. The forest cover will be increased by 270,00 ha
with an estimated incremental output of 11 million cm of timber and firewood. The
production of by-products will be significant. The main shrub species are caragana,
seabuckthorn, amorpha and H. mongolicum. At the end of the project the conserved area
of shrubland will stabilize at 250,000 ha. Since about 40 percent of the biomass can be
used as fodder, the forage production of leaves and branches will be about 140,000 tons
every year.
5.12        Grassland. Forage resources in the project area include planted grssland,
natural grassland, shrub forage, crop residues, processing products and feed grains. The
newly developed grassland area will be 155,000 ha, mainly of alfaf (Medicago sativa),
(Onobrychis satyra), Astragalus (Astrgus adsurgens) and sweet clover (Meilotus alba).
The yields per ha are about 3 tons for three-year-old alfalfa, 5-7 tons for two-year-old
Onobrychis, 3-6 tons for three year old astagalus and 3-4 tons for one-year-old sweet
clover. Through the establishment of improved grassland, two milLon tons of forage and
fodder will be produced annually.
C. MAURKEMG
5.13        As a result of increasing population pressres, the Loess Plateau has been
a deficit area for most basic food crops including grain and oil crops, unable to feed its
population without government support. Previous policies have emphaized food self



- 38 -
sufficiency, but have largely failed to achieve the desired effect, as farmers were forced
to produce grain on unsuitable steep slope land. Recent reforms allow farmers to diversify
into cash crop production, including fruit production and, as a result, enable them to invest
in capital land construction. This, in turn, increases their purchasing power and allows
them to diversify production even firther. Given the relatively moderate incremental
staple food production and the constant increase in demand as a result of population growth
(1.2 percent) and income growth (projected at 12.4 percent per year), there should be no
difficulty in finding domestic markets for grain crops and oil crops and meat. Increasing
export opportnities to both international markets and other provinces exist for cash crops,
particularly fruit. Import substitution, particularly for timber, is likely to be cost-effective.
The formerly rudimentary domestic marketing system is improving rapidly and is expected
to play a more important role in commodity circulation in the future. For example, there
are some auction markets for food crops, fruit, goats, catde, and timber in Xifeng (Gansu)
and Yanan (Shaanxi) cities and similar markets are developing in each subproject area
where production expands.
5.14        Crops. The former grain bureaus will remain buyers of grain to ensure
stategic supply to urban centers and pnce stabilization. They will procure about 10-
15 percent of marketable surplus. The remaining grain will be sold to a range of market
competitors. As famers become accustomed to markets, and planners gain confidence in
the overall balance of gmn supply and demand the activity of the bureaus will be fully
commercialized.
5.15        Thlber. The incremental production of timber would be absorbed by the
local provincial market, and through shipments elsewhere in China. Forest products
including poles and timber will be readily marketed in the local and provincial markets,
since the demand for these products is well above supply. In Malian (Gansu), tinber
imported from other provinces should still reach 15,000 cubic meters during the end year
of the project based on the 31 percent of total timber production impwted from other
provinces in 1992.
5.16        Apples. Apple production has expanded rapidly in China dunng the last five
years. Present annual output is 4.5 million tons, maldng China the third largest producer
of apples in the world. However, per capita apple consumption is only 4.1 kg cmpared
to a level of about 30 kg in the developed world. The growth rate in per capita apple
consumption after 1949 has been 7.8 percent annually. By the year 2000, per capita
consumption will be 10 kg annually. Domestic demand will exceed 13 million tons.
There is, therefore, a large market for good-quality apples in China, sufficient to absorb
incremental apple output that would be produced under the project. In 1991, apples were
sold through three channels. About 10-30 percent of the total production passed through
local govemment, 30-60 percent through wholesale traders from other provinces, and 20
percent through local farmers markets. Thus far, the largest sources of apples sold in the
project area to wholesalers are the Malian, Jialu, and Yanhe areas. In Malian, apples sold
to other provinces will consitute 50 percent of total production at the end year of the
project, up from 38 percent in 1992. In Weifen, 75 percent of the apple production was
sold in the domestic market in 1992. At full development, this will be 85 percent (6,100



- 39 -
tons), with the remaining 15 percent to farmers' own consumption. The major domestic
and regional markets are in Shanghai, Beijing, Tianjin, Guangzhou, Fujian, Shenzhen,
Hong Kong, Macao, and Southeast Asia.
5.17        The conditions on the Loess Plateau are partcularly suitable for apple
production for several reasons. During the first few years until production commences,
apple trees are typically intercropped with food crops, thus, the opportunity cost for the
land is minimal. At current market prices, the relatively low investment costs can be
recovere witiin two to five years after production commences. In periods of high market
prices, intensive maintenance and inputs can guarantee high incomes from small holdings
But even if prices should fall in the future, earnings from an average sized household plot
(0.1-0.3 ha) would still double or triple incomes of poor families. Apples not only provide
regular cash income, but they also add variety and balance to the farmers' diet. And in
penods of seasonal drought when gan yields are low, apple troes will st be productive
enough to provide cash income and food. Similarly, in periods when input supplies are
short, apple trees will perform for some time even without ferizer application. A further
advantage lies in the fact that effective and low-cost storage caves allow farmers to keep
apples for six to eight months without suffering a significant reducdon in product quality.
Thus, while concems about the impact of large-scale development of fruit trees on fuure
market prices are legitimate, there is little doubt that to the poor on the Loess Plateau the
expason of fruit production is one of the most beneficial developments in history.



- 40 -
6. BENEFIIS AND JUSTDIFCATION
A. BEraEFm
6.1        In addition to the incremental production described above, benefits wil be
substantial in four areas:
(a)   Poverty Alevition. The project attacks poverty at its core, as virally no
sources of income other than from agriculture are within reah for the
1.2 million people in the project area. The project will direcdy help to
allevie poverty in several of the poorest areas m China. Of the 21
counties in the project, 17 are clasified by the government as among the
poorest m China [pr capita income below Y 300 ($35)]. This includes all
countes in the Shanxi project area (Xn, Hequ, Baode, Pianguan, Xi, Pu,
Daning), all counties in the Shaanxi proect area (alu, Yulin, Ansai,
Yanchang, and Yanan), and Kuan, Qingyang, Ning, Zhengnin, Heshui
counties in Gansu. Data on the e!isting poverty situation are summaried
in A-nag", Table 1. About 2F ),000 households would direcdy benefit
from project activities. Ite nt per capita income of farm families who
adopt improved or new technologies under the project would be more than
doubled from Y 360 to Y 900 at full development. AnexJ, Table 2
presents a summary of the project's impact on household incomes.
0)   Downstream Benefits.  The sediment discarge modulus in the nine
watersheds ranges between 5,000 and 26,000 tkm2/yr resultng in an atnual
sediment discharge out of the project area of about 120 million tons. After
comprehnsive waterhed treatment this would be reduced by half,
contributing substantally to an improved sediment regime of the Yelow
River. Without large-scale counter measures, severe erosion will continue
to silt up the lower reach of the Yellow River as well as the resvoirs along
its way. The direct and indirect costs involved in dealing with 1.5 billion
tons of sediment annually (rising embankments, rduced reservoir lives,
silted-up irrigation canals, oppotnity cost of billions of cm of water
needed to flush the sediment into the ocean, ever increasing flood risk,
etc.), amount to sevemal billion Yuan annually. In the long run this problem
can only be dealt with at its sources by comprehensive soil and water
conservation on the Loess Plateau.
(c)   Environment. Iarge areas in the project area are severely degrded and
current agricultural practices are unsustainable. More than 100,000 ha of
presendy cultivated land will be taken out of production and, together with



- 41 -
an additional 300,000 ha of mainly wasteland, planted with a mix of local
tree, shrub and grass species. This will not only significantly reduce
erosion, but also improve the microclimate and reduce the flood risk in the
now often desert Mm gullies. The land use pattern will be adjusted for
agriculture and pennials, respectively, from the curent 28 prcent and
37 percent to 21 percent and 54 percent. The proportion of wasteland will
be reduced from 28 percent to 17 percent. Improved grassland management
will help to reduce the pressure on the existng grassland areas.
Furthermore, due to the serious fuel, forage and fodder shortages, several
hundred thousand ha of grassland are destroyed every year on the Loess
Plateau by farmers digging up grass sod to burn the roots. This practice
will be completely eliminated in the project area, due to increased supply
of superior, woody plant biomass.
(d) Employment opportunities generated by the project will help reduce
seasonal underemployment among the rural labor force in and around the
project area. The incremental quantity of labor days can be handled mainly
within the existing family structure.  In each subproject area, land
construction and prepartion would require additional hired labor which is
amply available from the underemployed labor pool throughout the year in
each province.
B. ECONOMIC ANALYS
6.2         Watershed rehabilitation proects with substantial environmental and
downstream benefits lend themselves only pardy to precise economic analysis. While it
is relatively straightforward to estimate economic costs and benefits of increased
agricultural output, it is more difficult to quantify the economic value of sediment control.
Recently concluded studies indicate that the cost to society of dealing with the tremendous
sediment load in the Yellow River downstream is so high that investments in the Loess
Plateau (the source of all Yellow River sediment) to reduce the sediment inflow into the
river can have a high economic return. An attempt is therefore made below to quant
the impact of the project on the sediment regime of the Yellow River, including the
corresponding reduction in downstream investments. It remains virtually impossible to
quantify positive externalities such as the drmatic increase in vegetation cover in the area
with the corresponding improvement in microclimate, increased soil moisture, cleaner air,
etc. These extemalities are therefore dealt with on a qualitative level below.
6.3         Economic analysis has been carried out for all major components (terracing,
warping land, irrigated land, forestry, orchard, and grass development) and for the project
as a whole. World Bank price projections or export prices actually realized in China were
takm to estimate economic farm gate prices in 1991 constant terms for traded inputs and
outputs. Conversion factors for nontradeable inputs were estimated and applied to convert
financial values to economic costs (Anne 2, Table 12.). Average costs for rail tansport
were estmated at Y 0.036 per kg, trucks and handling at Y 0.067 per kg. All economic
values were converted to local currency at the prevailing official exchange rate of Y 8.7



- 42 -
to $1. A conversion factor of 0.8 was applied for unskldled labor (other conversion factors
are summarized in Annx 8, Table 9, and a discount rate of 12 percent was used, which
is considered to approximate the opporunity cost of capital in China. All project costs
including investment costs, farm production cost and 5 percent physical contingencies, but
not price contingencies or taxes, have been included in estimating the economic viability
of the project.
6.4         Downstream Impact. The economic benefit of retaining sediment on the
Loess Plateau can be calculated in two different ways. First, by calculating the
opportnity cost of water that can be used for irrigation downstream instead of flushing
sediment (see below) into the sea. Second, if the flushing cannot be realized successfully,
by calculating the cost savings resulting from a delay in raising the Yellow River
embanknents (paras. 6.5-6.6). The recently completed Yellow River Investment
Study 1/ concludes that soil conservaion investments of up to $1.2 billion would allow
about 4 bcm of water-one sith of the volume now required annually to flush sediment
into the Bohai Sea-to be diverted for irrigation and other productive uses. Given the
increasing water shortage in the basin, the benefits based on an opporunity cost calculation
for water would be in the range of $280 million annually. If this calculation can be
confimed in further studies, this factor alone would justify the project on economic
grounds. But even if these assumptions turn out to be overly optimistic, the downstream
benefits clearly justify the investments in silt retention structures, which account for over
50 percent of sediment retention but less than 15 percent of total project costs. For the
economic analysis, it has been assumed that each ton of sediment retained on the plateau
would make 24 cm of water available downstream for urban, industrial or agricultual use.
The opportunity cost for 1 cm of water is very conservatively estimated at Y 0.04. Thus,
a benefit of Y 1 per ton of sediment retained can be added to the various project
components. The ERRs are presented in two columns with and without inclusion of the
downstream benefits (Table 6.1).
6.5         The long-term annual average sediment load entering the lower reach of the
Yellow River is about 1,500 million tons of which about 400 million tons is deposited
annually in the river channel. This sediment deposition raises the bed of the channel an
average of 10 cm per year and, as a result, the flood embankments along both sides of the
river have to be raised every 10 years. Construction of the Xiaolangdi Multipurpose
Project is soon to start at the head of the lower reach and is expected to be completed in
10 years. The reservoir will be opeated in such a way that it will store coarse fractions
of the sediment inflow (50 percent or 750 m tons annually) until an equilibrium position
will be reached in which the sediment will flow through the reservoir. The storage space
allocated to sediment deposition is sufficient to hold 7.5 billion tons. In the absence of
further erosion control measures in the Yellow River Basin, this storage space would be
filled in 10 years. During this period of storage there will be no sediment deposition in
the lower reach and there may be some scounng of the downstream channel. Thus, this
1/    Chin-YeLow River Basin Ivestmnt Plamning Study; Repot No. 11146-CA, Ibe World Bak
June 30, 1993.



- 43 -
storage of sediment will alow a delay of 10 years in the raising of the Yellow River dikes
and the associated river training works.
6.6         The project area of 15,600 kmn2 is drained by rivers which enter the Yellow
River above the Xiaolangdi Reservoir. This area has an average sediment modulus on the
order of 10,000 tons/km2/year. Thus, average annual sediment runoff with the existing
limited control measures is about 120 million tons. Of this total, 50 percent (60 million
tons) is the coarse fraction which creates problems in the Yellow River. The project will
virtually eliminate this sediment outlow from the project area. Over the next 10 years,
while Xiaolangdi is being built, other dams and land development works will also be built
in the main sediment-producing areas of the Loess Plateau and these will lead to a further
annual reduction of total sediment flow by about 300 m tons. Thus, the overall effect of
sediment control works will be to reduce the annual inflow of coarse sediment to
Xilangdi Reservoir by some 225 million tons. This will extend the filling period for
Xiaolangdi from 10 to 14 years. Furthermore, once the Xiaolangdi reservoir has reached
the equflibrium position, the annual deposition rate in the lower reach will fall to about 300
million tons from the current rate of 400 million tons; thus, the frequency of embankment-
raising campaigns would go to 13 or 14 years from the present 10 years. Each raising
canpaign costs about Y 3 billion in current prices. Delaying these investments, as
indicated above, results in a saving with a discounted value of Y 667 million at year 10
of the project. Thus, each ton of sediment (total sediment including the coarse fraction)
stored in the Loess Plateau has a discounted value of Y 1.5. For the purpose of this
analysis, the more conservative benefit of Y 1 per ton of sediment has been chosen in the
caculation.
6.7        The sediment trapped in reservoirs in the plateau will, in fact, deplete their
sediment storage capacity. Fortunately, the topography of the dam sites is such that the
height of dams can be raised periodically to restore their capacity. A typical dam would
have to be raised by about 5 m. The cost of raising the height of a sediment control dam
is much less than its initial cost, about 15 percent. This recurring cost is reflected in the
project cost stream for the purpose of economic analysis.
6.8        Rates of Retur. Based on the above assumptions and comparing costs and
benefits over a period of 30 years, the project's Economic Rate of Return (ERR) is
19 percent Crable 6.1). The ERRs range from 16 percent for the grassland development
component to 26 percent for the orchard component ass aU provinces. The smal
irrigation component in inner Mongolia shows a rate of return of 28 percent. At a
discount rate of 12 percent, which is considered to approximate the opporuity cost of
capital in Chima, and calculated over a period of 30 years, the net present value of the
project is Y 857 million.
6.9        Sensitivity Analysis. Sensitivity of ERRs for each of the project watersheds
and components has been tested for cost overruns, increased input costs and shortfalls in
output to identify the minimum adoption rates before the project becomes economically
nonviable. Table 14 in Aex 8 indicates that all investments in each component are
economically viable (with the exception of HBP watershed), even if investment costs

plantings) than other components, appear quite robust in terms of sensitivity analysis as

the combined ERR of a comprehensively treated watershed below acceptable levels.
6.10         Incomes. To assess the impact of the project on household incomes in the
project areas, a representative set of detailed household models was prepared. In each
subproject area three typical farm households were selected from three different income
stra  (igh, middle, and low income; samples are given in Azma8, Table 8). Tle farm
model analysis shows a substantial increase in household income as a result of the changes
in land utilization, cropping patterns, production technology, and consumption. The
project would double or triple the incomes of adoptees in many villages where



- 45 -
comprehensive control measures were implemented. This increase in incomes is achieved
despite various agricultual taxes, special goods tax, land rent and the project loan
repayment (16-20 percent of the total expenditure of individual households in each
subproject area). The standard charge for these items in each subproject area is presented
in &nnex , Table 12. The fam model analysis (examples from Xinshui watershed)
results indicate that the annual per capita income in a representative village household will
increase from Y 280 to Y 654 at full development for the poorest 20 percent of households
(Annex 8, Table 5), from Y 365 to Y 434 for the 65 percent of middle-income households
(Annex 8, Table 6), and from Y 450 to Y 852 for the highest-income 15 percent (income
above Y 450) of households at full development (Annex 8, Table 7). The financial budgets
for all households representing different income levels are summarized in Annex 8, Table
2, which shows an annual real income growth rate at full development of 11.2-27 percent
for the low-income households, 6.4-15.7 percent for the high-income households, and 5.1-
21.2 percent for the middle-income households. In particular, the establishment of a small
fruit orchard on most farms will result in a manifold increase in household incomes in the
medium term. In households without frit orchards, incomes will increase by about
50 percent.
C. RISK
6.11       The project is based on years of Chinese experience in soil conservation and
agriculture on the Loess Plateau. It is also based on the careful analysis of the WFP Mwi
Project in Shaanxi and the Bank financed component in Dingxi County, Gansu, which have
been quite successful. All components have been exhaustively tested, thus in its technical
aspects the project has few serious risks. The risk of inadequate management capability
in the project entities is minimized as experienced management teams have been in place
since the early stages of preparation, and further strengthening would be provided under
the project. As the project operates in four provinces covering an exceptionally large area,
strong supervision is required from the Yellow River basin authority represented by
UMRB. This is ensured through the agreed project management structure and would be
further enhanced through UMRB financial control over project funds. The risk of
unsatisfactory cooperation among the different line departnents at the provincial level is
minimized through the existing powerful project leading groups at various levels.



- 46 -
7. AGREEMENTS ANM RECOMMENDATION
7.1         The following agreements were obtained at negotiations. The Borrower
and/or the Project Provinces will:
(a)   conduct trainirg under the Institutional Development Component in
accordance with programs agreed with IDA. To that end, each project
province shall prepare, and submit to the CPMO, for forwarding to the
Association by November 30 of each year, a proposed program of training
activities to be caried out during the following calendar year (para. 3.18);
0)   ensure that the project will comply with agreed procedures and technical
standards for review of design, construction and maintenance of hydmu ic
structures (pam. 3.24);
(c) maintain separate accounts for the project and furnish the audited project
accounts, statements of expenditures, and Special Account to IDA within six
months after the end of each fiscal year commencing in 1994 (para. 3.37);
(d)   ensure that all pesticides used under the project shall have been approved by
the Asciation, and that the storage, handling, distribution and use of such
pesticides shall follow guidelines saisfctory to IDA (para. 3.41);
(e)   implement the part of the Environmental Monitoring and Management Plan
applicable to the respective project province in a manner satisfctory to the
Association (pam. 3.42);
(t)   ensure that CPMO and PMOs at provincial, prefecture, county and township
levels would be maintained with functions and responsibilities acceptable to
IDA and staffed by competent staff in adequate numbers (pam. 4.4);
(g) ensure that the provinces and the CPMO will maintain, or cause to be
maintained, Technical Panels wjih memberships, functions and
responsibilities acceptable to IDA (para. 4.5);
(h) ensur. that charges will be collected from the water users for the irrigation
systems completed under the project at levels sufficient to cover full annual
operational and maintenance costs (pam. 4.8);
(i)   ensure that for participating households the minimum land contract terms in
the project area will be 30 years for orchards, teraces and warPing land;



-47 -
all other land will have a minimum contract period of 50 years; and that al
farmers affected by the project wil receive wnitten and signed contracts, and
that copies of such contracts will be held in the village or township
registries and will be available for inspection (para. 4.10);
U)   ensure that the provincial PMOs would prepare and submit, through CPMO,
a detailed annual budget and work plan to IDA for review by November 30
of each year in respect of project implementation the following year
(para. 4.11); and
(k)   ensure that the CPMO would undertake jointly with provincial PMOs and
IDA a mid-term review of the project by September 30, 1997 (pa. 4.20).
7.2        The approval of the Credit Agreement by the Borrower's State Council
would be a condition of credit effectiveness.
7.3        Subject to the above, te proposed project would be suitable for a credit of
SDR 106.3 million ($150 million equivalent) on standard IDA terms to the People's
Republic of China.

LAND DEVELOPMENT AND EROSION CONTROL
1.         Background. Since the creation of the PRC there have been several
campaigns in the Loess Plateau, especaly in the 1970s, involving terracing, afforesttion,
and silt control dams. In the past, the purpose of these measures was seen mainly as soil
conservation and litte attention was given by planners or farmers to maximizing production
and incomes. The lib   on of the agriculture sector in the 1980s and the growth of
a free market has given farmers more incentives to exploit the resources of the plateau.
While individual efforts are now rewarded by the market, the development of the Loess
Plateau calls for carefully organized, collective efforts to cope with the highly complex
land forms and land capabilities, and the sm.-',' wrea of arable land (about 1.5 ha) available
to a farm family. Thus, a new element which tas been introduced in recent years is the
comprehensive and integrated planning of individual watersheds. The objective is to create
sustainable production of field crops and orchards on high-yielding level farmland and
thereby replace the areas devoted to crops on erodible slope lands, and plant the slope
lands to a range of trees, shrubs and grasses for the production of fuel, timber and fodder.
The area devoted to crops is reduced, but the per ha productivity is greatly increased by
proven techniques of soil and water conservation. While the watershed is the basic
physical unit (often divided into subwatersheds) the village is the basic social unit, and the
success of the integrated approach depends on close consultation with the village leaders
and village members. In recent years, this integrated and comprehensive approach has
been successfully tested and implemented on a large scale in a number of watersheds in
the plateau, and it has proved to be sustainable.
2.          Table 1 lists, by province, the areas proposed for the various forms of land
development and the number of dams. Terraces, afforestation, and grassland for a total
of 510,200 ha account for most of the land development proposed in the project Its main
purpose is to enhance the productivity of the land in an environmentally sustainable
manner. While land development also reduces sediment runoff, the primary means of
sediment control are the dams constructed in the gulLies under this project. All land
development and erosion control works would be built according to technical standards
summarized in a Technical Standards Manual (TSM).
3.         Planning of Comprehensive Small Watershed Development. Chapter 1
of the Technical Standards Manual (TSM) describes the objectives and methods of
comprehensive watershed development and provides the foundations for rtional evaluation,
development and use of the land resource on a sustainable basis. The determiion of
slope, its direction and length is critical to comprehensive planning on the Loess Plateau.
Slope effects soil losses due to erosion, soil moisture and fertility levels.  Site
investigations would include desk analysis of topo, soil, sedimentation and erosion, land



- 7 -                        ANNEX 3
use, agncultural and other maps, as well as a review of data available at research and
monitoring centers. Standard requirements would include:
*    transect surveys, sample investigations would vary, but generally minimum
sample areas would be: 1- 4 m2 for herbal plants, 10 - 20 in2 shrubs; 400 nil
for trees, over 25 in2 for surface erosion, and 1 km2 for determining over
all erosion losses.
*    maps would be from 1:5,000 to 1:25,000 scale and would include land type,

agricultural land use, and current land development and conse.vation works.
These would al be located on one or more maps and would be entered into
subwatershed GIS.

erosion status.
- t he subwatershed investigation report would be checked by Prefectural and
County PMO staff. A minimum of 5 percent of the area investigated would
be spot checked in the office and if necessary in the field, checking would
include itemized and categorized areas, measurement accuracy, and
statistical data. A minimum of 80 percent accuracy would be required as
an acceptancy level.
4.         Subwatershed plans would meet the broad Regional social and economic
plans, as well as the micro needs of the subwatershed population. Soil and water
conservation planning would treat the watershed as the planning unit. The main planning
asks would be to determine the ratio between farm, forestry, and pasture lands, their
location, and the distribution of land development and conservation measures. A key
planning principle wuld be ta where possible all land on slopes of more than 25e woud
be taken out of annual cultiion and replaced by perennial fuit, tree, shrub and pasture
species. Other principles include:
*    engineering, revegetation and agricultural practices would be fully
integrated;
*    conservation measures would normally be initiated on slope land and then
followed by measures in the gullies, main gullies, and lower river reaches;
*    subwatershed planwi should fully realize and optimize economic cosbenefits,
ensure acceptable reurns on investment, with very apparent conservation,
ecology, and social benefits.
S.         Subwatershed planning teams would comprise leaders from local authorities
and villages, and professional staff from all key departnents working with nahtral
resoures in the area Plans would include:

*    analysis of cunrent land use conditions;

6.          Terraces. Ihe conversion of slope lands to terraces serves a number of
purposes. First, it creates a better environment for crwp producton through moisture
conservation; in a year of average rainfall, grain yields on terraces are more than throe
times higher than on slope lands. Second, terraced land is more resistant to damaging soil
erosion during intense storms than slope land. And third, sediment runoff is much lower
from teraces than from slope land. Geeally, terraces are cnfined to lands where slopes
are less than 250. Over most of the Loess Plateau the soil is deep and is therfre well
suited to terraing, but the topsoil is often set aside and replaced upon completon of the
tenaces. Land close to villages have pnority for terracing. Uniform standards for
plannmig design and construction have been developed in reent years and will be adopted
throughout the project. For a typical terace on a slope the terace width would be about
8 m, the iser would be a 2 m high, and the earthwork quantity per hectare would be
around 3,000 cubic meters (cm). All terraces would be longitudinally and horizontally
level, except in the case of irrigated erraces where longitudinal slope gradient should be
from 1:300 to 1:500. The terrace lip would be designed to retain a designed rainstorm
event completely. The ndge dry bulk denity would be 1.4 tons/r. Teraaces should have
appae drainage and erosion prevention featres  Terrace ridges shod  be kept
consolidae  and their crest shoud remain level. ridges should be checed after



-79 -                                 3
rainstorms, and should be protected with suitable grasses and shrubs. The construction
cost ranges from Y 3,000/ha to Y 7,000/ha depending on the slope.
7.          Sediment Control Dams. There are two types of sediment control dams:
key dams and warping dams. Key dams are located close to the outlet of subwatersheds
with catchment areas of 3-5 kmn. The initial gross storage capacity of a key dam is
generally between 0.5 million cubic meters (mcm) and I mcin, but it can be as high as 5
mcm. Warping dams are located on smaller tributary catchments upstream of the key
dams; their initial gross storage capacities are generally less than 0.5 mcm. Key dams
serve several functions: sedinment retention, flood control, and storage of water for
irrigain and village water supply. A key dam is normally built first on a watershed as
part of development plan which provides for smaller warping dams on the upstream
tributaries soon after the completon of the key dam.
8.         'Warping' is a term used to denote the formation of land by the
accumulation of river-bome silt. It can consist of trapping silt in a reservoir or the
diversion of nver water into an offstream setting area. The purpose of warping dams is
to mtercept sediment and thereby create land, and at the same time they preserve the
storage capacity of the downstream key dams. A system of key dams and warping dams
in a subwatershed provides 100 percent control of the sediment runoff.
9.         In the Yellow River Basin, sediment runoff from an area is expressed as
tons/km2/year (the sediment modulus) and is averaged over a number of years because
tere is a wide variation in soil erosion from year to year. The parts of the loess plateau
with the highest sediment runoff have a modulus of about 20,000. This is, however, an
average of runoff from a wide variety of tenain and land use. Researchers have found that
typically about 70 percent of the soil loss is from the gullies and about 30 percent from the
surrounding land.
10.        Design and construction standards for sediment control dams have been
improved considerably following some dam failures in 1977 when exceptionally intense
stom hit the loess plateau. The dams are homogenous earth-fill embanknients built in the
deeply incised ges of the loess plateau. The areas submerged by the dams cosit of
eroded slopes and gully beds and are unihabited. In recent years a mode of construct
using hydraulic fill has been perfected. The loess material on the sides of the gully is
sluiced with high pressure hoses into the dam body. The fill is contained between
upstream and downstream shells formed by bulldozers. The lcess forming the foundafion
for the dam is cleared of loose material in the gully bed and the abutments before the fill
is placed.
11.        A notable feature of the smaller dams is that most of them are built without
spillways. At most sites it is cheaper to size the dam and the reservoir to store the enfire
volume of the design flood than to provide a spillway. The design standards call for key
dams to be designed for a 30-year flood and checked for a 200-year flood. A 30-year
flood is in fact the volume resulting from a 30-year storm. This volume has to be
contained without exceeding a reservoir level 3 m below the dam crest. The flood from



- 80-                         ANL 
a 200-year storm has to be contained with a reservoir level 0.5 m below the crest. A 30-
year, 24-hour storm in the loess plateau depends on location and ranges between 100 and
160 mm and a 200-year storm ranges between 140 to 210 mm. This design approach is
conservative since the significant flood-reducing effect of dams that have been built, or
may be built, upstream is ignored in the design of the key dams. Thus failure of the
upstream warping dams would not endanger a key dam. The active storage capacity for
the dams is the gross capacity less the volume of sediment estimated to be store in 15
years. Thus, in the years when the sediment is building up in the reservoir the actual
capacity of the reservoir exceeds the required design capacity, and it is during this period
that the upstream catchment is likely to be further controlled by additional sediment control
dams.
12.         The geometry of the gullies is such that key dams and warping dams have
to be relatively high to serve their purpose; the range is usually 5-15 m but some are as
high as 30 m. For most reservoirs, the bulk of the active storage volume is in the top 5
m and, when sedimentation begins to infringe on the flood storage capacity, the option is
available at most sites to increase the height of the dam. This can be done at a ftaction
of the initial cost since it involves a low embanknent placed on the sediment behind the
dam. At many sites, raising could be carried out a number of times since a dam typically
occupies a smail part of the total height of a gully. Each dam bas an outlet conduit with
an intake structure designed to accommodate future raising of the dam.
13.         The topography of the watersheds allows considerable flexibility in siting the
dams. Site selection criteria for the dams include a requirement that they should be located
at sites remote from existing or potential settlements. All the dams proposed for the
project satisfy that criterion. Therefore, in the unlikely event of failure there would be no
danger to life and limited damage to property.
14.         Key dams and warping dams are designed by the provincial water resource
bureaus according to standards developed by the Ministry of Water Resources in 1986
('Technical Standard for Major Gully Control Structures,' SD175-86). These standards
have been endorsed by the Planning Academy of MWR as well as the Ministry of Energy.
These specify aU aspects of design and construction including planning procedures, site
selection, dam body layout, calculation of hydrologic design parameters, foundation design,
choice of construction materials, construction techniques, hydrologic and hydraulic design.
15.        The procedures require multilevel technical reviews and approval of design
and construction of sediment control dams. Preliminary designs are carried out by county
water and soil conservation bureaus. Initial review and approval of stuctural design and
siting will be carried out by provincial water and soil conservation authorities who will,
in turn, submit construction applications to the Upper and Middle Reach Bureau (UMRB)
for final review and approval. The ultimate responsibility for ensurng compliance with
design procedures and technical specifications will rest with the UMRB.  Ihese
procedures, are currently applied to strces over 15 m height, but UMRB has agreed
to that they will be applied to all dams over 10 m height (Table 1).
