16.           Constuction is carried out by constnrction teams selected and supervised by
the county water conservancy bureaus. There are numerous such teams qualfied to
constuct dams using the hydraulc fill technique.  The advantage of this mode of
construction is that it produces a dense and uniform fiUl which is less demanding, in terms
of close quality control and inspection, than mechanically compacted fill.
17.           Routine m&intenance and inspection of strucures wiU be carried out by
townships and villages under a contract to the county water conservancy bureaus. These
contracts specify the type of maintenance to be performed and how often these procedures
should be performed. Provincial authorities wil also carry out inspections of each key
dam dunng the year. In addition to such routine maintenance, UMRB wiU conduct pre-
and post-flood season inspections of aU key dams and an information system is being
established by the provinces, with help from UMRB, which wil document their
consruction and maintenance history.



-82 -                         ANNX3
18.         Gully Control. In the project areas of Inner Mongolia and Gansu the gully
beds are narrow and lend themselves to control by check dams. These are low brush dams
built across the gullies at 20 to 30 m intervals. The effect is to slow the velocity of flow
during floods and thereby reduce undercutting of slopes which often leads to landslides.
Measures are also adopted, especially in the gullied plateau landform, to arrest erosion at
the heads of gullies. Shrubs are planted along the edges of the gully head, and small dams
are built in the gully.
19.        Irrigated Land. Irrigated land under the project is referenced to small
pieces of land, and design standards would be carried out according to small duty irrigation
and water conservancy engineering. The following cntena should be followtxl:
*     Surface irrgation systems should guarantee water a minimum of every other
year, i.e., 50 percent;
*     Canal network water use coefficient should not be less than 0.65 for gravity
schemes, and not less than 0.7 for pumped schemes;
*     Canal cross section, pump station and well irrigation design criteria are set
out in Chapter 4 of TSM;
*     Irrigated areas will be properly drained, drainage gradients, depending on
soil type will range from 1:300 to 1:500;
v    F-armers will be responsible for operating and maintaining irrigation and
drainage systems.
20.         Land Formation from River Bed Reclamation. Under this system part
of the river bed is reclaimed as agriculturl land. The system entails the construction of
protective dikes and the diversion of silt laden river water to provide the land fill. The
following criteria apply:
*     Design standards are such to withstand up to 20-year floods;
*     Height of dike above river bed level is generally no more than 3 m; dikes
should normally be earth with vegetative, stone, or wire net protection;
*     Construction should normally take place outside of peak flood season;
*     Details for irrigation or flooding of reclaimed areas are in Chapter 5 of
TSM;
*     Management and maintenance of reclaimed lands would be responsibility of
farmers. Reclaimed land, particularly the dikes, would be checked prior to
the flood season, any damages would be repaired.



- 83 -                        ANNE  3
21.         GuUy Head Prtection. Gully head protection (GHP) specifications are at
aCapter 6 of TSM. GIHP on the Loess Plateau is nornally in the form of a ditch and
ridge. The distance from the ridge from the gully should be 2 dmes the height of the
gully. Ridges should be on the contour. Trees, shrubs and grasses should be planted on
ridges. Some ndge and ditch systems may be constructed in conjunction with small
storage ditches.
22.         Check Dams. Check dams are small structures, less than 5-m high, that are
either impervious or pervious. The cross section of earth or rock check dams is generally
trapezoid. General engineering standards applicable to small duty conservation engineering
should apply. Vegetative check dams would be made of poplar and willow, and should
follow prescribed procedures (chapter 6). Management and maintenance would be by
farmers
23.         Afforestation. Some of the past programs of afforestation have produced
mixed results; there have been some notable successes, but in some areas survival rates
and growth rates have been low. Current practice is more scientific and solidly based on
field trials. More attention is now paid to suitng plantings to location, soils, slope
direction and gradient, and exposure, and to improving seedling quality. Tending and
protection has been improved by the "production responsibility system" which gives
individual farmers a financial stake in the survival of plantings. Afforestaion is based on
a detailed plan involving a classification system taking into account all factors relevant to
species selection. Tree species commonly used include chinese pine, poplar, black locust,
and paulownia. Shrubs include seabuckthorn, caragana, amorpha, and red willow. Cash
forests include apricot, walnut, and mulberry.
24.         Land preparation for forestry should meet minimum standards, and would
compnse contour planting (along the slope cultivation is banned) on slopes of less than
15 percent. On steeper slopes terracing (both level and backward sloping), level ditching,
and fish scale pitting would be used.
25.         Forestry data would be collected systematically and would be kept under
technical files that would be reviewed regularly by professional leaders. Further
specifications for forestry development would be included during implementation.
26.         The average daily fuel consumption is about 10 kg per household
representng little more than half of the requirement. This is mainly composed of straw,
other crop residues, dried grass roots branches and needles from the shrub and forest
areas. Large diameter wood is almost totally imported from other provinces. There is
need for construction quality poles and small diameter logs. For reforestation the
following slope criteria should be respected: Economic trees should be planted mainly in
the valley bottoms and on slopes of less than 25° and soils of more than 2 feet depth.
Timber plantation should be established preferably on slopes of less than 30 degrees.
Above these limits fuel and protection plantings of shrubs and trees should be established.



- 84 -                        ANEX 3
27.         The fuel shortage is 0.3 million tons every year. The accumulated timber
volume is 4.4 mcm. This is only 3.8 cm per capita or 1/21 of the world average or 2/5
of the national level, thus timber shortage is a protruding problem in the area. After
completion of the project the forest area will increase from the existing 210,000 ha to
about 480,000 ha. Commercial forests will be located in the areas where the sites are
suitable and the water content is sufficient such as on the plateau surface, the guly
bottoms, as part of the four-sided afforestation program, on the floodplains and on table
land. The suitable tree species are chinese pine chinese poplar, willow, locust, and
paulownia. Protection forest, conseaion forest and windbreaks are planted on steer
slopes, broken landforms, seriously eroded areas. The main species are locust, arborvitae,
seabuckthom, cagana, amorpha, etc. Fuelwood forests are planted mainly in the
Maianhe and Yanhe watersheds where transportation is inconvenient, and there is a lack
of fuelwood. Generally, the gentle slopes will be used for plant fuelwood forest, the main
speies being seabuckthom with large biomass production and rapid growth. For economic
rees the main species are prickly alh, mulberry and wuallt. ReLwrftod ares will hbe
pianted with a mix of species complementing each other in growth, nutrient requirements
and disease and pest prevention. The main patterns are mixed forests of locust and pine,
pine and poplar, poplar and elm as well as poplar and seabuckthorn. Afforestation
densities vary with the rainfall pattern, they are generaly low in the arid areas and for
commerci  forest and grzing forest; high for conservation forest and fuelwood forest.
Planting densities for the individual species are outlined in the technical specifications (see.
Annex 4).
28.         Seeding Nurseries. Nurseries are a central part of the afforestation
component. At present, poor quality seedlings are a major constraint. Seedlings show
poor trunk, shape, poor root systems, poor adaptability with the result of poor growth
performance in the past. Irrational seedling densities in the nurseries lead to weak
seedlings. During project implementation 1.2 billion seedlings will be produced and
planted. It is planned to develop 1-2 central nurseries in every county with 7 to 10 ha
each. These cental nurseries will produce about 15 percent of the seedling stock. They
will adopt advanced technologies and produce high-quality seedlings. The remaining
85 percent of seedling production will be contracted out to farmer nurseries and production
will be basetJ on similar technical standards. These farmer nurseries will be located near
the afforestation sites.
29.         Shrubs. Shrubs will be planted on about 163,400 ha, including 74,200 ha
of caragana (Caragana korshshnii), 56,100 ha of seabuckthorn (Hippophae rhamnoides),
18,700 ha of amorpha (Amorpha fruticosa), and 14,400 ha of desert willow (Salix
microstachya). Shrubs will be mainly planted on ridge tops, the upper part of north-facing
slopes, and under harsh ecological conditions, such as those encountered on the high-
altitude plateaus of the Inner Mongolia project areas. Besides providing fuel, raw
materials for particle board and baskets (desert willow), berries (seabuckthorn) and
livestock feed (namely caragana), shrubs will be used to stabilize edges of gullies and
highly erodible, steep slopy areas. Shrub pastures planted under the project are expected
to generate between 0.75 and 1.5 tons hay equivalent of relatively low quality forages per



- 85 -                        ANXI
year, with the highest yields being obtained from caragana and the lowest from desert
willow shrub pastures.
30.         Shrubs, such as seabuckthorn, together with black locust trees will also be
used for the planting of living hedges for subdivision of grazing areas. With the expected
large-scale granting of exclusive user rights for grazing land to individual livestock owners,
living hedges will become of utmost importance and, therefore, their planting will be
encouraged and promoted under the project. Plating of shrub will be done from May
through September with seedlings to be provided by nurseries to be set up in each County.
New shrub plantations will be protected for the first three years after establishment. As
with past  improvement, planting of shrub will be contracted out to farmers, who will
obtain exclusive, individual user rghts for the land they planted with shrubs.
31.         Plant Hedge Establshment. Hedges will be used for a number of purposes
using a variety of soecies that are available in the proiect arem including:
hedges on crop land using Achnatherum splendens, day lily, and tamarisk
would be primarily for soil conservation purposes. Hedges would be from
1-2 m wide and be planted in belts 10-40 m apart depending on slope.
These hedges would be primarily used as a soil and water conservation
measure;
a    hedges on grazing lands would be used for livestock control, for fuel and
fruits, and for protection from wind. The hedges would be up to 3 m wide
and would consist mainly of seabudcthorn, cryptotaenous cinquefoil, and
yellow rose;
*    hedges similar to those of grazing land would be used for forest protection;
*     oither hedges would be used as wind breaks and for stabilizaton of sand
dunes (using sand willow and seabuckthorn); as grazing hedges in belts
6-8 m apart (using caragana and seabuckthorn); hedges for gully control
(using seabuckthorn, splendid achnatherum, locust and oriental arborvita);
and for river bank and river bed stabilization in the form of revetments
comprising poplar, willow and seabuckthom.
32.         More detailed specifications for hedges will be developed during project
implementation as further experience is gained in this technology.
33.         Orchards. Chapter 8 of TSM sets out planning principles for orchard
development. Plans include site selection, sizing of orchard, and the planning of associated
infrastructure including roads, irgation (where available) and other structures.



- 86-
34.         Orchard fruit tree species require careful selection depending on location.
Generally in remote areas 60-70 percent of the trees should be late maturing. In hilly area
fruit trees should be planted on terraces. Planting densities vary according to site and
species. Details are in Chapter 8, Table 8.5.3. of the TSM.
35.         Planting of trees can be done either in late autumn (ending 20-25 days
before orchard soil freezing) or in the early spring. Pits should be 80-100 cm in diameter
and 100 cm deep. Fruit trees should be correctly ferilized with organic and inorganic
manures. Orchard hygiene is very important if high yields are to be achieved. Chapter
8 sets out systems that are to be used.
36.         Shelter belts for orchards are important featues, and their composition will
vary according to land type and ventilation requirements. The main tree species for shelter
belts include: poplar, dry willow, Chinese pine, locust, white elm, seabuckthorn, and a
nu.mbcr of 4"h%=   test1 e dpe cies. Shelter belts should be normaly of mixed species to
assure effectiveness at all heights of the belt. Belts should be from 15-20 m wide. Distance
between belts should be 400-500 m Planting densities for belt species should be 1-2.5 m
for trees, and 0.5-0.75 m for shrubs.
37.         Forage and Livestock Development. Between 1985 and 1992, livestock
populations in the project areas increased by 30 percent. More specificaUy, cattle grew.
by 28 percent, sheep by almost 40 percent, goats by 59 percent, pigs by 16 percent,
horses, mules and donkeys by 12 percent, and poultry by over 40 percent during that
period. But the strong growth in livestock populations since 1985 was not matched by a
corresponding growth in the livestock feed base, except for brans and stovers, the
production of which grew by resectively 58 percent and 34 percent between 1985/87 and
1991/92. Production of manufacured feed and oilseed meals remained relatvely stable;
and output of pastures has considerably decreased during the past 10 years as a result of
overgrazing due to increased livestock numbers.  Poor grassland management and
overgrazing has also led in many instances to depletion of good quality herbage species
(with consequent low digestibility values) and severe sheet and gully erosion. Data on the
extent to which pasture productivity and herbage quality have declined during the past 10
years do not exist; but declines in the order of 5 to 10 times are cited for Inner Mongolia,
while those of the Southern, higher rainfall project areas are believed to be much less
severe.
38.         Virtually all livestock in the project areas is owned by individual households.
Owning livestock, particularly cows (which can be kept as multipurpose animals for
production and sale of calves, provision of draught power, production of manure, and
capital accumulation) is of utmost importance to farmers in the project areas, as this is one
of the pnncipal means by which poor farmers can accumulate assets and increase their
incomes. The holding size of catte is generally 1 to 3, including calves; but there are also
larger herds with up to 15 to 20 head, and many households share a cow or a draught ox.
For goats and to a lesser extent for sheep, the extent and pattern of ownership is hily
dependent on the project area and altitude. There is a tendency to move from sheep to
goats and for individual flock sizes to increase as one moves from lower to hbgher altitude



- 87 -                        AN=  3
villages and into areas with harsher agro-ecological conditions. In low lying villages,
about 25 percent to 33 percent of all households typically own I to 5 sheep. At the other
extreme, in higher altitude villages, it is quite common that the majority of all small
ruminants belong to one or two farmers (often the former herders under the commune
system), who may own up to several hundred sheep and goats and, further, that the share
of goats in the herd can be as high as 75 percent to 100 percent. About 50 percent of all
households own one to several pigs. The degree of pig ownership in most parts of the
project areas is low by Chinese standards, indicating competition for feed with human
nutrition and draught animals. Many households which keep a draft animal cannot afford
to also raise a pig. By contrast, pigs are kept by households too poor to keep a draft
animal.
39.         Performance of livestock is low by international standards.  Average
slaughter weights of cull cows, sheep and goats are respectively only around 300 kg, 25
kg and 20 kg. First calving occurs at 3 years and first lambing at almost 2 years of age.
Fertility rates are equally low, oscillating around 50 percent for cows, 80-85 percent for
sheep and 85-95 percent for goats. Twin births in sheep and goats are rare. Mortality
rates, 8 percent and 15 percent respectively for calves and lambs/kits, are high when
compared to mortality rates of adult cattle and sheep/goats, which are in the order of
rVpectively only 2-3 percent and 4 percent. Pig fattening performance varies widely.
While average households can fatten pigs for sale at 90 to 100 kg in 9 to 12 months, poor
households may take twice as long. Chickens lay 60 to 100 small eggs per year (22 to 25
eggs per kg).
40.         There are about 375,000 ha of natural pastures and 17,500 of improved
pastures in the project areas. A further 254,000 ha is classified as shrub pastures and
another 210,000 ha as forests. To some extent, these two latter land categories are also
utilized for grazing and forage production. About 230,000 natural and shrub pastures are
above 1,000 m altitude, where vegetative growth rates and response to fertilization are
reduced. Sward composition of natural pastures varies considerably among project areas,
but can be illustrated by the following 3 typical flora:

Yields of natural pastures are low, amounting to no more than 0.75-1.20 tons of hay
equivalent per ha per year.
41.         Except for relatively smal areas of improved pastures around villages anc
limited afforested areas, all grzing land of the project areas is communally owned and
used by villages. As a result, productivity of grazing land has significantly deteriorated
during the past decade due to substantial buildup in livestock populations (particularly of
sheep and goats) and the prevailing gazing practices under which communally owned
lands are grazed by individually owned livestoc.L As in other countries with similar
grn  systems, such practices have led to severe overazing, soil erosion and declines
in herbage digestibility on viruly all project area pasures, as livestock owners tend to
grze as many animals as possible in an attempt to maximize their share in Xrofit from the
common grazing resource without reinvesting in pasture improvement and maintenance.
42.         The major objective of the proposed forage and livestock development
component will be a substantial increase in feed supply. Particular emphasis will be given
to increased availability and quaity of winter- and spnng feed and more rational utilization
of natural grazing land and improved pastures. Realization of sustainable livestock
production systems and higher productivity per livestock unit, while simultaneously
reducing overgrazing and soil erosion, will be the ultimate objective to be reached in the
project areas.
43.         The main technical thrust of the proposed forage and livestock development
component will be: (a) increased production of forages, both in terms of volume and
quality, from improved pastures and forage shrubs; (b) introduction of modern, intensive
forage crop production via adaptive research and trial work on farmers' fields; and
(C) disseminaon of more rational utilization of gzing land and improved pastures by
fostering the granting of individual user rights to livestock owners.
44.         A total of 155,000 ha of improved pastures will be established under the
project, including some 74,100 ha of fal (Medicago sativa), 56,800 ha of astragapus
(Astragalus adsurgens or erect milk vetch), 11,600 ha of sainfoin (Onobrychis satyra1),
7,900 ha of sweet clover (Melilotus alba), and 4,600 ha of odier forage plants, such us
Coronilla varia (crown vetch), Vicia villosa, Bromus inermis and Elymus sibiricus.



-89-                          AN3X
45.         The above major plant vaneties are well adapted to the agro-ecological
conditions of the prtect area, and will be planted over an eight-year period. Pasture
development under the project will be carried out on four types of land: (a) gently sloping
land and hill tops; (b) teraced, retired amble land, which normally is about 2-5 kn from
vilages and has been abandoned due to its remoteness and low yields resulting from
insufficieAnt manure application; (c) terrced land similar to the above but closer to vilages
and therefore, suitable for eventually returning to crop production after 4 to 6 years under
forage legumes to improve soil fertility and structure; and (d) land close to villages in
valley bottoms on which crop yields are relatively low due to water logging.
46.         Alfalfa will be planted mainly on gully bottoms, retired arble land, shady
slopes and flat hill tops; astragalus on ridges and sunny slopes; sainfoin on retired arable
land; and sweet clover on gully bottoms. Improved pastures will not be grazed during the
first year of estabLishment. Most suitable legumes, grasses and shrubs for project area
grzig land and recommended planting and sowing techniques are given in Table S. Zero
grazing ("cut-and-carry") will be encouaged, as it can increase effective forage and
manure yields by a factor of 2-3, compared to traditional grazing techniques. Exected
yields are in the order of 3 tns of hay equivalent for 3 year old allf, 3-4 tons for 3 year
old astrglus, 5-7 tons for 2 year old sainfoin, and 3-4 tons for one year old sweet clover.
At full development, improved pasures financed under the project are expected to generate
a total of about 500,000 tons in bay equivalent in years of normal rainfal, or the
equivalent of feed for about 800,000 sheep units. To allow however for fluctuations in
fodder yields due to drought, the above production figures will need to be adjusted
downwards by about one tbird.
47.         Pasture improvemen will be caried out by individual farmers, who will be
granted exclusive, individual grazing rights of up to 50 years for the land they improved
under the prqect.

PROJECT TECHNOLOGY AND TECHNOLOGY SUPPORT
T7he Project's water and soil conservation investments wiM be based on an interrelated
scientific and techical development program consisting of a project backd program of
research, demonstration, training and extension. Improvedfaming is not possible withot
a scentikfc basis ..... Prject Feasibility Report.
Iltroduction
1.         Very sig-ificant steps have been made in improving agricultural productivity
m the project area over the past decades. The improvements are due primarily to a
systematic approach in applying appropriate technology to soil and moisture conservation,
and to technical gains in varietal improvements of cereals and other crops. These
combined with an inherent capability of the farmers to absorb and modify technology as
needed have resulted in some impressive production increases. The preservation of soil
moisture in this dry area receives priority attention by farmers and service agencies, and
is achieved through cultivation practices based on contour farming techniques;
improvements in soil organic matter and hence moisure retention capity; creating new,
flat, and moisture retaining lands by warping systems that arrest sediment in valley bottoms
and alongside rivers; and the terracing of slope lands (up to 25') to reduce rainfll run off.
All these methods will at the same time reduce the tansporttion of sediment to down
stream lotons.
2.          The project area is well served by research and education institutions
particularly in Shaanxi and Shanxi Provinces. The project will therefore concentrate on
introducing and improving existing technologies through field experimentation and
demonstration, and will look to test new technologies and management pracdces that will
lead to a second generation of less costly conservation techniques and better managed
p nial trees and forages. The project wilU emphasize a comprehansive approach to
watershed development, one that has the objective of taling steep land (more than 25 ) out
of annual cropping, and replacing it with perennial trees, shrubs, and grasses. This same
approach allows for intensification of annual cropping practices in vatley bottoms on
warped lands, and on hillside terraces. The combination should lead to the doubling of per
capita incomes of participating farmers.
3.          Under ftis project, technology' means: (a) maintenance of technical
quality against agreed technical standards; () technology testng, exeimtation and
innovaton involving leading farmer technicians and professional engineers and scies;
(c) technology transfer and dissemination; and (d) training. The applicaton of agood
science based prctces' should assure the tecbnical risk is reduced to a minimum, and that



- 92 -                        ANNEX4
ir conjunction with agreed technical standards a sound basis will exist for monitoring
project execution and for the disbursement of loan funds. This backed by agreed
improvements in land leasing arrangements (minimum of 30 years for the best land) and
Ide jure' responsibility contracts should give farmers the long term secunty and eturn for
their hard work.
4.          Four provincial 'Technical Panels" (IP) and one at cental level (CT?) have
been established to provide a technical overview and a 'challenge" to project 'technology"
as described in para. 3. These TPs would have no executive authority, but would provide
guidance to the respective Leading Groups and PMOs on a wnde range of technical
matters that would be expected to be both challenging and innovative. These TPs would
also have an important rok in assuring an open exchange of technical matters between the
four project provinces and between the project and the rest of China (details in paras. 21-
23).
Teckmical Standards
5.          The project's early success will depend much on the application of currently
known and well tested technology. Later success, particularly in subsequent phases beyond
the scope of this project, will depend on the development and testing of new technologies
that are both more cost and conservation effective. The feasibility study describes most of
the existing technologies in detail. Most are backed by manuals of various levels of detil
and quality. In order to assure that technology (a) is consistently applied at high standards,
and (b) can be properly monitored, a Technology Standards Manual (TSM) has been
prepared. The manual is in two parts, the first dedicated to technology, and the second
to project opeatonal procedures. The manual bnngs together the key cnsrvation and
development technologies to be applied under the project. Using cunrent standards it sets
out the range of acceptable specifications to be applied for the engineering and biological
methods to be used under defined site conditions. Each province has slightly different
speifcations - these will be set out in provincial supplements to the TSM. The Manual
has been prepared by UMRB in conjunction with the provincial PMOs and wil be
reviewed by the Technical Panels prior to publication. TSM will be distributed to aU field
staff, and will thereafter be used for detailed planning, implementation and technical
monitoring. At the end of each year CPMO would review feedback from the provincial
TPs on technology application, and would modify technical standards where necessary.
Thus field staff would have access to a dynamic process of technical standards that are
both quantifiable and monitorable. Disbursements of IDA credit funds would depend on
monitoring reports based on the standards. A draft of the TSM has been completed and has
been reviewed by the Bank. A summary outline of the agreed technical standards is
presented in Annex 3. Note technical standards will include: engineering/ biological
designs, construction/establishment instuctions, timing/seasonal opportunities, labor /
machinery I equipment / planting material requirements, and an indication of unit costs.



- 93 -                        ANNE 4
Research and Experimentation
6.          The project area is rich in institutions; there are 50 scientific research and
teaching institutions, with more than 10,000 senior and junior technicians. Of these there
are 20 institutions dedicated to soil and water conservation employing more than 1,000
scientific and technical staff. Many of the institutions have been awarded commendations
for standards of excellence and innovation. The project will not duplicate research under
taken by other agencies, and particularly will avoid financing basic research, such research
falls under the current responsibilities of the Ministries of Agriculture and Forestry and
institutions such as CAAS. Neither will the project provide general funding to MWR,
YRCC, or provincial institutions undertaking research in soil and water conservation.
Rather it will direct and contract such institutions to undertake research and
experimentation in the field in conjunction with farmers in order to meet the needs of
fanners and subsequent generations in the project area, and in technical and managerial
areas that are clearly deficient. The following areas will receive special attention during
the life of the project: (a) crop protection and IPM techniques, particularly for the apple
and fruit components; (b) soil fertlity and the use of fertiity enhancing techniques such
as nitrogen fixing rhizobium and mycorrhiza; (c) green manuring and composting, the use
of crop by-products and cultural technologies (deep plowing, seeding techniques, etc);
(d) forestry and fruit tree nursery techniques; (e) tree and orchard planting and
management techniques; (f) forage crops, forage grasses and legume establishment and
management techniques; (g) livestock management, livestock control, livestock reduction,
and substitution on grazing lands, and pen feeding of stock; (h) shrub management and live
fence development; (i) the use of vegetative contour barriers on sloping lands as a low cost
method of soil and water conservation; () improved methods in terrace and dam
construction and their maintenance; and (k) social, economic, and technical
interrelationships in comprehensive watershed development. Table 1 of this annex sets out
ur more detail the experimental features that have been agreed.
7.          Approximately Y 10 million has been allocated at provincial level to support
project research. These funds would not be used for the direct purchase of labory
equipment. Complimentary research at provincial level rather than compedtive progams
(see Table 2) will be supported. This table indicates research topics identified by the time
of appraisal, with priority topics in "bold" pdnnL The TP, would annually review all new
research proposals, see how they fit with current research efforts, and would agree with
the PMOs the best locations for research with the objective of getting research results
quickly and efficiently. The TP would also review the progress of aU ongoing research,
and would eliminate programs that fail to have an effective output or are unliely to
produce further cost effective results. Each research program will be backed by design
details, location, and cost (rable 6 shows a sample experimental log). This data wiU be
recorded in the GIS program at county level. The data will be monitored, and will be
reviewed annually.
8.         Initialy research will focus on key selected sites that are representative of
the project area and the technologies and problems to be investigated. Results from these
sites will then be further tested and demonstrated in other selected areas of the project,



-94-AR                                4
prior to being generally disseminated to the townships and villages at large. Experimental
and demonstration sites will normally be located on a farm under the management of a
certified farmer technician. Each site will have a 'site committee" made up of the farmer
technician and other selected farmers (in China farmers have always played an important
role in field experimentation) who will be reponsible for undertaldng periodic reviews of
the experiment / demonstration and for writing the observations in the experimental log
book. Primary expermental sites will be under the full responsibility of the contracted
agency undertaking the research, and a similar site review and assessment process will be
established. The TPs at central and provincial levels will review annually the site log
books, so that appropriate lessons and actions can be taken.
9.          An important feature of the project would be the sending of small teams
(farmer technician, researcher and ,)rofessional project staft) to search and find" existing
technologies that may be available at research stations in other areas of China, that might
have direct or indirect relevance to project needs. Some of the technologies may be
directly transferable, others may need testing and demonstration first. This 'search and
find" would for some technologies be extended to overseas visits to carefully identified
technology areas.
Technology Transfer and Dissemnation.
10.         The feasibility study sets out the principles of technology transfer. It must
be assumed that the county technical services will handle, as at present, annual crop
technology transfer. From the high standard of crop production it appears that the existing
extension system is adequate. The project will therefore mainly focus on technology
ansfer relating to soil and moisture consrvation technologies, fruit and forest trees, and
livestock and forage development. Farmers need good and improved technical information
rlating to: nursery management, orchard and woodland mnent, gsand
establishment and manamement,   b       ment, livestock management and forage
production, crop protection  M), land cultivation, and fertlity management. These topics
will be covered by the project 'Technical Standards Manual" that will be available to all
village farmer technicians, and will be backed by specific technical hand books for wide
distribution to farmers and technicians. Pamphlets to update these handbooks will be
prepared and distributed from time to time. Soil and Water Conservation Technicians at
village level and above would receive technical reorientation training at the beginnig of
the project and thereafter would attend annual technology workshops to provide two way
feedback on current technology and its modification (where necessary).
I1.        The more progressive farmers, often acting as Village Technicians, with
demonstrative leadership capabilities, are responsive to technical training - such training
will be provided in relevant and specalized areas, and the custom of awarding
"Certificates' to proven performers will be maintained. In China individual farmer
training has proven to have a good pay off, and the project would use this, in conjuncdon
with relevant demonstradons, as a key to technical improvement.



-95 -                         ANEX 4
12.        The use of video, slides, radio and TV will be used to back up technology
extension. The technical input to audio/visual media will be improved. Technicians with
a bent towards this work will be matched with good cameramen and designers so that
technically acceptable and practical products are produced. Audio/visual methods need to
be locally adapted where possible - farmers like to seethear familiar faces, voices and
scenes in technical presentations. CPMO will design an effective way of evaluating the
results of technology transfer; this will be done annually, and the evaluation will be
reviewed by the Technical Panels. The maximum use of contracting the production of
technical audio/visual aids will be made, and consideration is to be given to contracting
"Certified" farmers and other specialists to provide technical advisory services to farmers.
13.        The pro.ct will spend about Y 13 million on technology transfer. These
funds will be used for (a) the introduction of new varieties and techniques from within
and outside China, development of demonstration sites, and technical transfer activities
(publications, video, etc)-19 percent; (b) hiring of technical consultants and contracting
technology transfer specialists/agencies to undertake specific technical transfer activities-
45 percent; (c) construction of technical centers at County level-13 percent; and

reasonable. There is some overlap between research demonstration and technology
demonstration and dissemination. Where possible the TPs will assure that there is a

14.        Each year the PPMOs will on the advice of their TPs prepare, review and

following year. The strategy will be dyn3mic and would be responsive to priority needs.
The TPs would also review technology transfer progress.
15.        China has a good reputation in the field of agrculture and farmer education.
Technologies offered to farmers generally produce good results if applied corrctdy; in
consequence there is a good learning culture amongst farmers and technicians. Because
of the hierarchal nature of the project organization, training at various levels is not
difficult. As a result literally tens of thousands of farmers (many of whom attend night
school) can benefit from project training. Training will be caried out at CPMO, PPMO,
prefecture, county, township and village level. Project training will provide the means for
farmers, technicians, and administrators to achieve the objectives of the project and thus
will strictly focus on need. Training will take place in China and overseas. During the
life of the project it is estimated that 260 policy makers and senior managers, 2,240 middle
level managers and key techniciar' and 131,000 farmer technicians will receive training
of an average of about one month ch.
16.        Higher level managers and policy makers will be trained in strategic
planning, management, and project adminition. Professional technical leaders will
receive additional training to update their technical knowledge in light of the project's

Technical Standards and their modification, in improved watershed planning and design,
in ecology, agriculture, hydrology, sediment control, and aids to planning and monitocing

have high levels of theoretical knowledge, and professional and experienced technicians
who can benefit from short training courses or carefully selected site investigations, and
from attending relevant conferences. Some 10 topic subjects have thus far been identified
(see Table 4) involving some 12 countries that are leaders in the technologies. Overseas
taining will total about 260 person months.
18.        CPMO will design its training program to meet the annual needs of the
project. PMOs will establish priority training needs for all components of the project.
Training curricula will be modified each year following a review by the provincial and
central TPs. In making the necessary modification account will be taken of the annual
modifications to the project's Technical Standards Manual, of staff and farmer response
to training (determined by an annual evaluation of trainee feedback), of training impact in
the field with regard to better design and application of standards, and of suggestions by
the Technical Panels. Success will also be determined by the quality of instructors. Senior
professionals and technicians who have demonstrated competence in project implementation
will be used part time as instructors. Funds are available to hire specialists in specific
topics, such as nursery and orchard management, from non project agencies or consultants.
Under the responsibility contract system some 'private' instructors may be retained as
training contractors with set goals and would receive payment by result. This system
would work well for customized computer training, accountancy training, Ilntegrated Pest
Management in orchard crops, advanced agricultural practices such as drip irrigation, on
farm moisture conservation management, hot house winter vegetable production,
senculture production, and the introduction of new forages such as grain amaranth, forage
maize and sorghums, and the use of soil fertility enhancing methods involving the use of
plant hormones, rhizobium, and mycorrhiza. There are some excellent scientists and
teachers at the universities and agricultural institutes who could be contracted for these
training assignments.
19.        To reduce construction and overhead costs, as well as to improve the overall
management of the project at various levels, it has been agreed that, where possible,
training center buildings will form part of the project's administrative complex.



- 97 -                        AhN.U
Technical Assistance (TA)
20.         Technical Assistance from non project agencies, such as CAAS, and
consultan;ts within China is required and is included under the research, technology
transfer, and training components. Areas where foreign TA would be of value would be
in forestry and orchard nursery and field management, in new computer applications-GIS
in particular, and in pasture and forage development. One of the best ways this could be
provided would be through participation, by proven and experienced specialist, on the
project's Technical Panels. It is the intention of the project to retain such expertise twice
a year for a total of four to six weeks. The specialists would be expected to provide a
medium term commitment to the project and would provide an independent view of project
investments and technology, as well as providing specific recommendations In their area
of spealizaton. These specialists would be funded by the proect or through bilateral
agreements with donor agencies. Bilateral agencies that might prove useful would be
Australia for forest nursery and orchard management, UK for IPM technology, New
Zealand for forage development, livestock, and forest nursery management, USA and the
Netherlands for soil and water conservation practices, and for soil fertility maintenance,
and the USA for GIS. There are also a number of University consortia that would be
interested in expanding their ties with the project in areas of research. The Bank would
help the project put some of these bilateral arrangements together during the process of
project implementation. The Bank itself has a good deal of experience in the areas
mentioned above, and Bank staff would from time to time be expected to share their
knowledge, and come up with innovative approaches, particularly in the use of computer
technology, and in this latter area would continue to support where possible the training
initiative started during preappraisal and appraisal.
Technical Panels (TPs)
21.         TPs have been establshed at provincial PPMO and at CPMO levels. The
TPs will work to well defined and already agreed terms of reference. The main tasks of
the TPs will include the periodic (twice a year) review of project technologies and their
applications in their broadest sense including quality, transfer and expertise of staff. They
will (a) assure that technology specifications are continually updated, (b) review and
comment on annual research plans, (c) provide innovative suggestions and ideas external
to the province, (d) review the effectiveness of technology dissemination practices,
(e) assess the effectiveness and adequacy of training; and (t) generally act as a sounding
board for the advancement of. ;w techniques.
22.         At PPMO level the PTP wil consist of representatives of the provincial
Techrkical Committees and knowledgeable and credible professionals from relevant agencies
extemal to the project (who could act as extemal peer reviewers). In particular, experts
on forestry, orchard, grasses and forage production, pest control, and soil fertility
maintenance will be included. The PTP would prepare annually an independent report on
the standard of application of technologies, and the relevance and effectiveness of the
research programs. At CPMO level a TP will be established that would have a similar
function to the provincial TPs, and would have the special responsibility of providing a



-98-                                   4
complete project overview with regard to technology and its application. The Central
Technology Panel (CTP) will comprnse of a technical representative from each Province,
representatives from CPMO, and external peers of repute. CIP will also rtain two or
three foreign ronsultants as members. The foreign consultants wil provide special
expertise in soil and water .onservation, forestry, orchard and grassland/livestock
development, and forage production. They will be hired for approximately four to six
weeks per year, initially over a period of three years. Apalt from responsibilities similar
to the PMPs, CTP will advise CPMO on the need for retaining technical expertise to deal
with some specific aspects of the project; for annually reviewing reseah progkams and
technical standards; and for training and overseas visits of project staff. The Chairpersons
of TPs will be independent of the project administation.

-112 -                         ANNEXs
PROJECT MANAGEMEN INFORMATION SYSTEM
Introduction
1.         The objectives of this project are: (a) to improve farm incomes through land
development and improved farm practices; and (b) to promote soil and moisture
conservation practices with a view towards reducng sediment flows to downstream areas.
These objectives will be achieved through application of proven land improvement, soil
conservation, and land rehabilitation technologies at the subwatershed and farm levels.
The project's strategy is to intrduce comprehensive land development in approximately
1,040 subwatersheds-each averaging about 12 lan2 (1,200 ha). These subwatershjeds are
distributed over 21 counties and municipalities across four provinces (Gansu, Shanxi,
Shaanwi, Inner Mongolia). Within each subwatershed a variety of land development
activities are envisioned including: the construction of teraces; the construction of vanous
silt retention dams; the estblishment of tree plantaions and orchards; the improvement of
grasses and shrubs; and the upgading of agriculural management methods. In a typical
subwatershed project activities will be phased over a period of three to five years.
2.          Given the large number of subwatersheds, their wide physical distribution
and the unique nature of each subwatershed treatment plan, an innovative project
monitoing system is considered crucial. An effective monitoring program will enable the
Bank to reimburse against expenditures for completed activities that meet the quality
standards laid out in the project's Technical Standards Manual (SM). In addition, a
systematic monitoring system wiU be indispsble for revisng annual work plans for each
subwatershed during the project implementation period. In order to address the special
monitoring needs of the project, a comprehensive approach to monitoring has been
recommended. Ihis approach which will cover all aspects of physical progress tracking,
financial accounting, implementation scheduling, envionmental benefits assessment, and
socioeconomic impacts assessment
3.          Monitoring Physcal Achievements and Progres Reportig. The project
monitoring system will be designed to record basic financial and physical progress data
required to implement the project effectively. Each project management unit will be
responsible for tracking the flow of information on resources (staff, equipment, money)
utilized under the project. The project management offices will penodically report on the
progress of project implementation including actual accomplishments versus planned
accomplishments, and actual expendite versus planned expenditures.
4.          Monitoring of Socioeconomic Benefits. The monitoring program will link
physical progress and financial monitoring with monitoring which evaluates impacts on
local communities and individual beneficiaries. The project will depend on two sources



-113-                           ARM S
of data to determine social and economic changes. First, it will use data that is collected
annually by the statistical authorities on economic changes. Second, it will undertake
representative household surveys and field investigations to measure the impact with and
without the project. Special attention will be given to measuring changes in output due to
investments in land development and to the new technologies introduced to the project. At
the same time household investigations will measure the impact of the project on increased
household wealth, and improved access to social services. This data will be rtained in
the project's overall information system (see following section) thus allowing close
corelation between investments, their quality and cost, and social and economic benefits.
Because investments will be identified in detail, i.e., it will be possible to know precisely
for each site location the investment costs, management practices used, annual yield data
etc. It should be relatively easy to undertake analysis of different sites and extrapolate that
data to the watershed as a whole. This information would then support household and
other surveys.
5.          Monitoring Sediment Control and Other Environmental Benefits. One
of the most important objectives of the project is to stabilize highly erodible landscapes in
order to reduce the off-site costs of downsram soil erosion and sediment depostion.
Thus, the project will undertake regular sudies that will analyze the impact of project
actities on sediment transport reduction and its downstream benefits including its impacts
on poverty reduction. All four provinces will monitor changes in water quality, sediment
transport reduction, on-site eroson, pecipiton, and changes in soil fertlity. In total 188
rinfal stations, 37 hydrological sins, 10 water quality stations, and 191 water
sampling and tracling stations will be used for monitoring environmental changes. Many
of the stations already exist, others will be added under the project.  The large
hydrological stations are manned permanently. The project will provide additional
trumentation to allow effective measurement and coUlection of data, some automatic
measuing devices will be installed in remote areas. Hard daxa from collecting stations will
be reinforced by the use of satellite imagery and mobile field teams that will under take
special investigations at selected sites. All the data will be entered into the project's GIS
data system.
6.          The UMRB, and the provincial and county Soil and Water Conservation
Bureaus are experienced in the data collection envisaged under the project. Data will be
collected on a continuing basis and periodic analysis will be undertaken to track changes
that occur. Selected subwatersheds will receive special attention in order to identify early
trends in environmental changes. Past monitoring of subwasheds under comprehensive
treatment have provided the basis for developing the expected benefits of this project, and
they are substantial. The projects PMIS will continue the process, but on a wider scale
and will assure regular feedback to the Provincia and UMRB managers and technical
panels for consideration in the review of research and technical standards. The project will
also assess the impact of nonproject road construcon, mining, and industial and civil
constrction as point sources for eroson and sediment, and their impact on overall
sediment flows in the project areas. Selected sites for monitoring will also reflect the
different typs of Loess topology using paired (treated and nontreated) subwatersheds of
about 10 km2 each.



- 114 -                            =       S
The Project Ma eent Iforation System (PMS)
7.          In addition to supporting the field work required to gather information for
monitoring putposes, the project will support the establishment of a computer based Pojet
Management Information System (PMIS). A PMIS is envisioned which will be based on
an integrated set of personal computer software applications that will failitate the
management of financial, temporal, and spatial information.j/
8.          OrganIzation and Management of the PMIS. Ite PMIS will involve a
hierhy of monitoring activities at the subwatershed, county, and povincial levels
(Figure 1). The central PMO will have oval responsibility in seeing that the provinces
carry out effective monitoring and that standardized methodologies are used. The centra
PMO will prepare an annual monitring report for the entire project area. At the
provincial level, monitoring will be caried out at the prefecur and county leves and will
be sumnarized by the Provincial PMO. Where necessary provincial PMOs will contract
with other agencies and instittions to undertake special studies, particularly in the
sociological and economic areas. Each year the monitoring plan and feed back will be
reviewed by the technical panels.

9.          SchelIg and Ilme Management. Overl project scheduling and time
management will be handled using a software application such as MicroSoft (MS) Prqject.
Each level of project management-Upper and Middle Reaches Bureau (1), central PMO,
four provincial PMO, prefecure offices (optional), and 21 county PMOs-will be atpected
to establish such capabilities. A separte implementation schedule (file) will be set up for
each subwatershed. The subwatershed files will be treated as subprojects of the overall
county Project File to permit aggregation to the county level.
10.         Tables 1 and 2-generated by MS PrcK,ct-illustrate sample schedules of
acdtvities to be camried out by the central PMO (UMRB) and the Shaanxi Province PMO.
The schedules are backed by P.E.R.T. charts (not shown) that demonstrate activity
linkages. They show how planned and rescheduled activities can be tracked as
demonstrated in   ement delays. Table 3 is a sample implementation schedule for
Ansai County, one of five counties and m4uniciplites participating under the Shaani
Province component of the project
11.        The county PMIS will be cntical to the oall success of the PMIS concept.
It is at this level that subwatershed monitoring takes place, and where GIS and accounting
information is linked togedter (see following sections). Table 4 is the work plan schedule
for Lijiagou Subwatershed based on project planning data The schedule (an -MS Project'
subproject of Ansai County) is backld by resource use, cost, and time scheduling data.
Each task is identified by a unique job code Qinked to the accounting system and to GIS).
Table 5 (MS Project) summanzes critical Subwatershed data and show more precisely
when expenditures are to be made and against which job. This data can be transferred to
the financial and budget planning applications (Quicken), if required, on a monthly basis.
Table 6 (MS Project) summanzes planned labor and other resource use on a quarterly
basis. By using special resource calendars, subwatershed investment work can be
automatically excluded from months when climate or other competitive operations are not
conducive for project development. Table 7 (MS Project) relates to the resources in Table
6 (MS Project) but in this case shows their planned cost.
12.        Flndal Mnae         . The second aspect of the PMIS is the intoduction
Of financial accounting and cash flow traclkng software. All expenditues and other
finandal transactons will be recorded in a financial management system which can
generate summary reports by expenditure class, by location, by payee and so on. Table
3a shows a year 1 budget report for Ansai County. Tbe subwatershed investments are
recorded in an individual 'asset account' shown at the bottom of the table. Table 3b
demonstates cash flow analysis for the county, Table 3c summarizes the total county
assets including site investments at two subwatersheds, and Table 3d summarizes all the
labor contracts let thus far. (It should be noted that these four tables are illustrative model
based on hypothetical expenditures.)
13.         As the project develops, actual costs and resource use will be entered into
the financial database. Tables 8 and 9 demonstrate typical subwatershed accounting data
that are held in the county account. These data enable deailed, on-site verificaion of
contract costs. Each subwatrshed will be set up as a separate asset account, so in the case



- 116-                         ANNEX S
of Ansai County there would eventualy be 43 subwatershed accounts, all of wbich will
reflect actual costs for identified investment actvities. By using the system as outined
above, the project will be able to keep track of investment and de;velopment actvities at
all levels in the management hirarhy.
14.        Geographic Information Systems. The thirn element of the PMIS is the
use of desk-top Geographic nformaion System (GIS) technology. As part of the complete
package a GIS will be established at the county and provncial PMO levels. In general,
the GIS will be used for two purposes: (a) to store and retrieve maps and associated
tabular information describing the initial conditions of each subwatershed; and (b) to
record, on a standardized map base, information illustrating the project's physical
achievements as they are completed in the field. The GIS envisioned for this purpose
should be simple to learn, inexpensive to purchase, and should be capable of running on
commonly available PC systems.
15.        The use of GIS will be closely interlinked with the financial management,
the computerized project management, ad other database management software available
in the PMO. Iitially, a complete, stand-alone GIS databa  will be establised for each
subwatershed. Each county will establish standardized datsets for all subwatershed under
tir jurion.  Ultimately, aUl of the individual subwatershed daftsets will be
ageted into four large unified database at the provincial level.
16.        The county level GSwill be used to support a number of specific
monitoring functions. First, GIS will be used to assess and record the peproect
conditions in each micro-watershed m order to help determne the suitability of portions
of the watershed for various treatment models.  ap 1 illustrates the existing baseine land
use conditions of a sample subwatershed. GIS can also be used to develop digital tenain
models PM  to identify key landscape characteristics such as slope, aspect, and existng
land use as a basis for evaluating various land use options. Map 2 displays the use of a
DTM to identify all areas well suited to orchard development. Map 3 illusates the
idealizd land use master plan which has been developed on the basis of land use,
landscape characteristics and land capability surveys as well as consideration of the social
and economic conditions. Table 10 was generated using GIS and shows the two-
dimensional "change-matrix" for the land use plan. The GIS thus becomes the formal
record of preproject land use conditions as aU field surveys and maps will be recorded in
the GIS.
17.        Second, GIS will be used to prepare and document the spaial dimenions
of the implementation schedule defined for each subwatershed. A separate GIS layer'
will be creatd for each project year and each project activity. A unique 'Job-I]D code
will be assigned to each land treatment area which can then be linked to progess reporting
and expenditure trackdng. These layers will illustrate the iming of the idelized land use
plan for the watershed. The GIS will be used in concert with the CPM software to ident
where, when and by whom physical implementation is expected to take place.



-117-                          ANNI
18.         Map 4 illustrates a hypothetical sequencing plan for Tree Plantation
activities derived interactively using GIS functions. Similar GIS data layers will be
developed for other activities such as terace construcdon, and construcdon of key,
warping and check dams. The map portrays the location and tming of planned opeaons
and indicates the unique 'Job-EDO code assigned to a specific work tak In addition to
the map the GIS will retain tabular data describing the salient characstis of each
treatment model including technical details such as land preparation requirements, fertlize
and pesticide application rates, and planned expendituues.
19.         Third, GIS will be used to record the location of completed works along
with tabuar information describing the as built chaistics. At periodic intevals in the
project (quarterly or semiannualy) maps and tabular reports describing the physical
progress within each watershed will be generated from the system. The GIS will be used
to record actual achievements against the baseline conditions of the watershed as the basis
for comparative assessment during project implemenation. As work is completed in the
field the achievements will be recorded into the GIS. Map S illustrates a hypothtcal
quarterly progress map showing plantation establishment completed in the first quarter of
project year 1. Finally, Map 6 illusras an example of an 'as-built progress map of
plantadons established by the end of Project Year 1. These maps can be used to alert the
project manager as to delays in implementaton and will form the basis of revising annual
work plans in subsequent project years.
20.         GIS at the provincial level will be used to aWegate all information received
from the counties into a master project dataase. The provincial GIS team will develop
methods and standards for collating all the individual databases into a comprehensive
databse showing progress across all individual subwatershed. These aggregated databases
will for the basis of upervion reports which will be presented to UMB Project
Magement Office.
